Nasafi's Refutation of al-Jubba'i on Divine Attributes
Nasafi refutes al-Jubba'i by demonstrating the self-contradictory nature of his reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jubba'i's argument for God's attributes through the perfect act leads to a logical contradiction as shown by Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i's own premises force him to accept the very conclusion he denies.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi takes on a specific argument from al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazili theologian. Al-Jubba'i claimed that the perfect act in the seen world indicates the agent's attributes of knowledge and power. Nasafi shows that this very reasoning, when applied consistently, must also indicate the same attributes in the unseen—otherwise, al-Jubba'i contradicts himself and opens the door for atheists to deny the Creator entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: conditional statements and consequences.
2
Identify al-Jubba'i's concession
Find where al-Jubba'i conceded that the perfect act is evidence for the agent's knowledge and power in the seen world.
This is the foundation of Nasafi's critique.
3
Follow Nasafi's application to the unseen
Nasafi argues that since the same kind of act exists in the unseen, it must also indicate the attributes there.
The key is consistency between seen and unseen.
4
Understand the consequence of inconsistency
If al-Jubba'i denies the implication in the unseen, he contradicts himself. Nasafi points out this opens the door for atheists.
This is a strong rhetorical point.
5
Consider the reversal argument
Nasafi suggests that al-Jubba'i's position can be reversed: one could claim the act only indicates the agent, not the attributes, in the unseen.
This shows the instability of al-Jubba'i's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول – وبالله التوفيق.
إن الجبائي بقوله إن الفعل المحكم المتقن يدل على ما كان له فاعل عالم قادر، سلم أنه في الشاهد دليل على الشاهد دليل على العلم والقدرة، وسلم أن ما هو دليل العالم القادر دليل العلم والقدرة وقد وجد في الغائب ما هو دليل العالم القادر، وكان ما هو دليل العلم والقدرة موجودا فيدل عليهما أيضا كما دل على العالم القادر على أنه لا حاجة بنا إلى إثبات كون دليل العلم، بل بنا حاجة إلى إثبات ما هو دليل العلم و القدرة وهو المفعول المحكم المتقن وقد سلم وجوده فالقول معه بعدم المدلول مناقضة وفتح أوسع باب للمبطلة في نفي الصانع مع وجود دليل ثبوته ثم لا ينفصل هو ممن يعكس عليه، فيقول : هو في الغائب دليل كون الفاعل عالما قادرا ودليل ثبوت قدرته وعلمه، وفي الشاهد دليل كونه عالما قادرا لا دليل علمه وقدرته. أو يقول: هو في الغائب دليل ثبوت علمه لا دليل كونه عالما بل هو الأولى، فإن
So we say – and with God is success. Verily al-Jubba'i, by his statement that the perfected precise act indicates that its agent is knowing, powerful, conceded that in the seen it is evidence for the seen, evidence for knowledge and power, and he conceded that what is evidence for the knowing and powerful is evidence for knowledge and power. And what is evidence for the knowing and powerful has been found in the unseen, and what is evidence for knowledge and power exists, so it indicates them as well, just as it indicated the knowing and powerful. So that we have no need to prove what is evidence for knowledge, rather we need to prove what is evidence for knowledge and power, and that is the perfected precise act, and its existence has been conceded. So the statement with him denying the implication is contradiction and opens the widest door for the invalidators in denying the Maker despite the existence of proof of His existence. Then he is not distinct from one who reverses upon him, saying: In the unseen, it is evidence for the agent being knowing and powerful and evidence for the affirmation of His power and knowledge, while in the seen it is evidence for him being knowing and powerful, not evidence for his knowledge and power. Or he says: In the unseen, it is evidence for the affirmation of His knowledge, not evidence for him being knowing, rather that is more fitting. Then...
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is the author's (Nasafi's) refutation of al-Jubba'i's position. The author argues that al-Jubba'i, by conceding that the perfect act is evidence for the agent's knowledge and power in the seen, must also concede that the same act is evidence for the same attributes in the unseen, otherwise he contradicts himself. The author points out that this inconsistency opens the door for atheists (al-mubtila) to deny the Maker. Furthermore, al-Jubba'i's argument can be reversed: one could claim that the act in the unseen is evidence for the attributes but in the seen only for the agent's being knowing and powerful, not for the attributes. The passage ends abruptly, but the core critique is clear: al-Jubba'i's position is self-defeating.

Key Terms
الجبائي
المفعول المحكم المتقن
الدلالة
العلم
القدرة
المناقضة
المبطلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Nasafi agrees with al-Jubba'i's argument.
Correction: Nasafi is refuting al-Jubba'i by showing that his own premises lead to the opposite conclusion and that he contradicts himself.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would result from accepting al-Jubba'i's position without the author's correction?
A) It would close the door for atheists.
B) It would lead to a clear affirmation of God's attributes.
C) It would open the widest door for the invalidators (al-mubtila) in denying the Maker.
D) It would align with the views of al-Ka'bi.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's own logic, when applied consistently, proves the very attributes he sought to prove, and his inconsistency only aids the atheists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the perfect precision in creation—the order of the stars, the complexity of a cell, the balance of day and night—do you truly let that evidence deepen your conviction that God is all-Knowing and all-Powerful? Or do you take it for granted, like al-Jubba'i, acknowledging it but living as if it means nothing? Name one specific sign you will deliberately contemplate this week and commit to a time for it.
?
Al-Jubba'i's inconsistency opened the door for atheists to deny the Maker. In what areas of your own life are you inconsistent—professing belief in God's attributes but acting as if they don't matter? What will you change starting today so that your actions match your claimed belief?
?
You have learned that the same evidence in creation points to both the Creator and His attributes. Which attribute of God—such as His knowledge, power, mercy, or justice—do you most neglect to acknowledge in your daily decisions? Write one concrete action you will take tomorrow to honor that attribute.

Refutation of al-Jubba'i on the Perfect Action
Author refutes al-Jubba'i by showing inconsistency between witnessed and unseen implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jubba'i's concession about the witnessed world forces him to accept the same implication in the unseen, and how his inconsistency opens the door for atheists.

Core Idea
Consistency demands that evidence for God in the seen applies equally to the unseen.

Al-Jubba'i granted that the perfect action in the witnessed world is evidence for the agent's knowledge and power. But he denied the same implication in the unseen. The author here exposes the contradiction and shows that such inconsistency only benefits those who deny the existence of God. This passage teaches us the principle of uniform implication in the argument from design.

Guided Path
1
Recognize al-Jubba'i's concession
Notice that al-Jubba'i admits the perfect action in the witnessed world is evidence for knowledge and power. This is the starting point of the refutation.
The phrase 'سلم أنه في الشاهد دليل...' indicates his concession.
2
Identify the contradiction
Al-Jubba'i then fails to accept that the same action indicates knowledge and power in the unseen, despite conceding that the same evidence exists. The author calls this a contradiction ('مناقضة').
3
See the consequence
The contradiction opens the door for atheists ('المبطلة') to deny the existence of God even though evidence exists. This is a serious charge.
4
Understand the inversion argument
The author shows that al-Jubba'i cannot avoid the argument being turned against him. If he says the action indicates attributes only in the unseen but not in the witnessed, or vice versa, his position is still inconsistent.
The inversion is presented as a hypothetical counter-argument that al-Jubba'i cannot escape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الجبائي بقوله إن الفعل المحكم المتقن يدل على ما كان له فاعل عالم قادر، سلم أنه في الشاهد دليل على الشاهد دليل على العلم والقدرة ، وسلم أن ما هو دليل العالم القادر دليل العلم والقدرة وقد وجد في الغائب ما هو دليل العالم القادر، وكان ما هو دليل العلم والقدرة موجودا فيدل عليهما أيضا كما دل على العالم القادر على أنه لا حاجة بنا إلى إثبات كون دليل العلم، بل بنا حاجة إلى إثبات ما هو دليل العلم و القدرة وهو المفعول المحكم المتقن وقد سلم وجوده فالقول معه بعدم المدلول مناقضة وفتح أوسع باب للمبطلة في نفي الصانع مع وجود دليل ثبوته ثم لا ينفصل هو ممن يعكس عليه، فيقول : هو في الغائب دليل كون الفاعل عالما قادرا ودليل ثبوت قدرته وعلمه، وفي الشاهد دليل كونه عالما قادرا لا دليل علمه وقدرته. أو يقول: هو في الغائب دليل ثبوت علمه لا دليل كونه عالما بل هو الأولى، فإن الإحكام دليل ثبوت العلم، ثم كان العلم بالعالم من ضرورة انتفاء ثبوت العلم بالعلم، لاستحالة قيامه بذاته.
Indeed al-Jubba'i, by his statement that the perfect, well-made action indicates what it had for an agent knowing, powerful, conceded that in the witnessed it is evidence for the witnessed evidence for knowledge and power, and conceded that what is evidence for the knowing, powerful is evidence for knowledge and power, and that which is evidence for the knowing, powerful has been found in the unseen, and that which is evidence for knowledge and power exists, so it indicates them as it indicates the knowing, powerful, in that we have no need to prove that it is evidence for knowledge; rather we need to prove that which is evidence for knowledge and power, which is the perfect well-made effect, and he conceded its existence. So to say along with him that there is no indication is contradiction and opening the widest door to the invalidators in denying the Maker despite the evidence for His existence. Then he cannot separate himself from one who inverts the argument upon him, saying: It is in the unseen evidence for the agent being knowing, powerful and evidence for the existence of His power and knowledge, while in the witnessed it is evidence for him being knowing, powerful, not evidence for his knowledge and power. Or he says: It is in the unseen evidence for the existence of His knowledge, not evidence for Him being knowing; rather it is more appropriate, for consummate precision is evidence for the existence of knowledge; then knowledge of the knowing is from the necessity of negating the existence of knowledge of knowledge, due to the impossibility of it subsisting by itself.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Jubba'i. The author argues that al-Jubba'i concedes that the perfect action in the witnessed world is evidence for knowledge and power, but he denies that it does the same in the unseen. The author points out that this is a contradiction and that it opens the door for atheists to deny the existence of God despite the evidence. Further, the author shows that al-Jubba'i cannot avoid the argument being inverted against him: if he says the action indicates attributes only in the unseen but not in the witnessed, or vice versa, he is inconsistent. The author concludes that the perfect action is evidence for the existence of knowledge, and the knowledge of the knowing is a necessary consequence of that evidence.

Key Terms
شاهد
غائب
دليل
مناقضة
مبطلة
محكم متقن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The perfect action indicates the agent's attributes only in the witnessed world, not in the unseen.
Correction: The source argues that if the action is evidence in the witnessed, it must also be evidence in the unseen, because the evidence (the perfect action) is present in both. To deny it is a contradiction.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jubba'i concede according to the author?
A) The perfect action is evidence for the essence only.
B) The perfect action in the witnessed world is evidence for knowledge and power.
C) The perfect action in the unseen is evidence for a state.
D) The perfect action indicates nothing.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's inconsistency in applying the same evidential standard to the seen and unseen leads to a contradiction that undermines his own position and aids the deniers of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about God, do you apply the same logical standards to Him as you do to His creation, or do you unconsciously lower the bar for the unseen? Where in your own thinking might you be guilty of the same inconsistency you condemn in al-Jubba'i? What will you do today to train yourself to think consistently about the Divine?
?
The author warns that inconsistency in theistic arguments 'opens the widest door to the invalidators.' Reflect on a time when your own careless reasoning about God weakened your faith or made you vulnerable to doubt. What specific step can you take this week to strengthen your evidential thinking about God's attributes?
?
Al-Jubba'i conceded the premise but rejected the conclusion. Is there a truth about God you intellectually accept but fail to apply in your life? If God's knowledge and power are proven by the perfect action, why do you still worry as if He is not in control? What is one situation this week where you will consciously rely on that proof to trust God?

The Refutation of al-Ka'bi on the Indication of Perfect Action
Author interrogates al-Ka'bi using a dilemma about what the perfect action indicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a perfect action cannot indicate a bare essence and must instead indicate an essence with a distinct attribute, establishing the Maturidi position on divine attributes.

Core Idea
An action's perfection points to a distinct attribute, not just a bare essence.

This passage presents a clever dilemma aimed at refuting al-Ka'bi, who held that the perfect action of an agent indicates only the essence itself. The author forces al-Ka'bi to choose between two options, each leading to a dead end unless one accepts that the action indicates an essence qualified by an attribute that is distinct from the essence. This argument is foundational for the Maturidi doctrine of divine attributes as distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text carefully, then the literal translation. Let the structure of the dilemma come through.
Notice the interrogative tone and the logical flow.
2
Trace the dilemma's two branches
The author presents two possible claims: (1) action indicates essence alone, (2) action indicates an essence qualified by an attribute. For each, follow the refutation carefully.
Pay attention to how the second branch leads to a further sub-dilemma about the attribute.
3
Recognize the application to the unseen
The author concludes by applying the same reasoning to God (the unseen) based on the uniform implication of evidence.
This establishes the principle that attributes are distinct in God as well.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للكعبي : ماذا تزعم ؟ أتزعم أن الفعل المحكم دليل على الذات فحسب، أم على ذات موصوف بصفة؟ فإن قلت: هو دليل على الذات فحسب. فهو فاسد، لأن مطلق وجود الشيء لا يدل على صحة كونه فاعلا، فإن الجمادات والأعراض موجودة وليست بفاعلة ولا بصالحة لحصول الفعل منها. وإن قلت: إنه دليل على ذات موصوف بصفة . قيل : هذه الصفة راجعة إلى الذات أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن قلت: راجعة إلى عين الذات عاد الكلام الأول، وصار دالا على مطلق الذات، وقد أبطلناه. وإن كان راجعا إلى معنى وراء الذات فقد ثبت ما قلنا وبطل سمعه، وإذا ثبت هذا في الشاهد ثبت في الغائب لأن مدلول الدليل لا يختلف الشاهد والغائب.
And it is said to al-Ka'bi: What do you claim? Do you claim that the perfect action is evidence for the essence alone, or for an essence qualified by an attribute? If you say: It is evidence for the essence alone, then it is false, because the mere existence of a thing does not indicate the correctness of it being an agent; indeed inanimate objects and accidents exist but are not agents nor fit for the occurrence of the action from them. And if you say: It is evidence for an essence qualified by an attribute, it is said: This attribute is referring to the essence or to a meaning beyond the essence? If you say: It is referring to the very essence, then the first statement returns, and it becomes evidence for the bare essence, which we have invalidated. And if it is referring to a meaning beyond the essence, then what we said is established and his hearing is nullified. And if this is established in the witnessed, it is established in the unseen because the implication of an evidence does not differ between the witnessed and the unseen.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi. The author asks a dilemma: either the perfect action indicates the essence alone, or an essence qualified by an attribute. The first is false because mere existence does not imply agency. The second leads to the question of whether the attribute is the essence itself or something else. If it is the essence, it reverts to the first. If it is something else, then the author's position (that attributes are distinct from essence) is established. This reasoning applies equally to the witnessed and unseen.

Key Terms
ذات
صفة
ذات موصوف بصفة
معنى وراء الذات
شاهد
غائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A perfect action indicates only that the agent exists, not that the agent has any distinct attributes.
Correction: The author shows that if it only indicated existence, then any existing thing would be an agent, which is false. Therefore, it must indicate an essence with a distinct attribute.

Checkpoint
In refuting al-Ka'bi, if the perfect action indicates a qualified essence, the attribute must be ______.
A) the essence itself
B) a non-existent state
C) a meaning beyond the essence
D) a separate creation

Key Takeaway
The perfect action indicates an essence with a distinct attribute, not a bare essence, and this holds for both human and divine agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate God's actions in your life—like provision, guidance, or trials—do you see them as evidence of His mere existence, or as evidence of His specific attributes like mercy, wisdom, and power? How does your answer change how you respond to Him right now?
?
If you were to treat God's attributes as identical to His essence, would that reduce Him to a bare abstraction? What would that mean for your personal relationship with Him? Commit to one concrete way today to acknowledge a specific attribute of God in your prayer.
?
The lesson argues that the implication of evidence is uniform between the witnessed and the unseen. Where in your own life have you failed to apply the same reasoning about God that you apply to human actions? What would it look like to be consistent starting today?

The Well-Made Act Proves an Attribute Beyond Essence
Dilemma refuting al-Ka'bi on implication of well-made act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the well-made act cannot imply mere essence but must imply an essence with an additional attribute, and how this argument refutes al-Ka'bi.

Core Idea
The well-made act forces us to affirm an attribute beyond mere essence.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi challenges al-Ka'bi's claim that a well-made act indicates only the agent's essence. By presenting a precise dilemma, he shows that either the act proves nothing about agency or it proves the existence of a distinct attribute. This argument is foundational for establishing that divine attributes are not identical to essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of a dilemma to force al-Ka'bi into a contradiction.
2
Identify the two main options
The passage presents a dilemma: either the well-made act indicates only the essence, or it indicates an essence with an attribute. Find where these are stated.
3
Analyze the refutation of each option
First, the 'essence only' option is refuted because mere existence does not imply agency. Second, the 'essence with attribute' option is split: if the attribute is identical to essence, it reverts to the first problem; if it is distinct, the argument succeeds.
This shows that al-Ka'bi cannot escape affirming a distinct attribute.
4
Understand the analogy between witnessed and unseen
The passage concludes that what holds in the witnessed world (shahid) must also hold in the unseen (gha'ib) because logical implications are universal.
This is a standard kalam argument from analogy.
5
Reflect on the significance for divine attributes
Ask yourself: How does this argument support the view that Allah's attributes (like power) are real and distinct from His essence?
This connects to the broader theology of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال للكعبي : ماذا تزعم ؟ أتزعم أن الفعل المحكم دليل على الذات فحسب، أم على ذات موصوف بصفة؟ فإن قلت: هو دليل على الذات فحسب. فهو فاسد، لأن مطلق وجود الشيء لا يدل على صحة كونه فاعلا، فإن الجمادات والأعراض موجودة وليست بفاعلة ولا بصالحة لحصول الفعل منها. وإن قلت: إنه دليل على ذات موصوف بصفة . قيل : هذه الصفة راجعة إلى الذات أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن قلت: راجعة إلى عين الذات عاد الكلام الأول، وصار دالا على مطلق الذات، وقد أبطلناه. وإن كان راجعا إلى معنى وراء الذات فقد ثبت ما قلنا وبطل سمعه، وإذا ثبت هذا في الشاهد ثبت في الغائب لأن مدلول الدليل لا يختلف الشاهد والغائب.
And it is said to al-Ka'bi: What do you claim? Do you claim that the well-made act is evidence only for the essence, or for an essence described by an attribute? If you say: It is evidence only for the essence, then it is false, because the mere existence of something does not indicate the validity of its being an agent, for inanimate objects and accidents exist and are neither agents nor suitable for the occurrence of action from them. And if you say: It is evidence for an essence described by an attribute, then it is said: This attribute is it referable to the essence itself or to a meaning beyond the essence? If you say: It is referable to the essence itself, then the first argument returns and it becomes indicative only of the absolute essence, and we have already invalidated that. And if it is referable to a meaning beyond the essence, then what we said is established and his hearing is nullified. And if this is established in the witnessed, it is established in the unseen, because the indication of evidence does not differ between the witnessed and the unseen.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's claim that the well-made act (al-fa'l al-muhkam) serves as evidence for the agent. The author presents a dilemma: either the act indicates only the essence (dhat) of the agent or it indicates an essence with an attribute (sifah). If al-Ka'bi says it indicates only the essence, that is false because mere existence does not prove agency; inanimate objects (jamadat) and accidents (a'rad) exist but are not agents. If he says it indicates an essence with an attribute, then the question is whether that attribute is identical to the essence or is a distinct meaning (ma'na). If identical, then it reverts to indicating mere essence, which has been invalidated. If distinct, then the author's position is established and al-Ka'bi's view is refuted. The argument concludes that what holds in the witnessed world (shahid) also holds for the unseen (gha'ib) because the indication of evidence does not differ between them.

Key Terms
الفعل المحكم
الذات
الصفة
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The well-made act proves only that the agent exists.
Correction: The act proves both existence and that the agent has an attribute (power) that makes agency possible, otherwise mere existence would not distinguish agents from non-agents.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why does the mere existence of something not prove it is an agent?
A) Because all existing things are agents.
B) Because inanimate objects and accidents exist but are not agents.
C) Because agency requires a specific attribute.
D) Because evidence for agency is always hidden.

Key Takeaway
The well-made act points to an attribute distinct from essence, establishing that divine attributes are real and additional to the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the order and precision in your own life—your body, your circumstances, your trials—do you see only a vague Creator, or do you see an intentional Agent with specific attributes? If you have been content with mere belief in existence, what is it that keeps you from letting that well-made act press you into affirming His mercy, justice, or wisdom as real and distinct? What would change in your du'a today if you truly believed each attribute is a separate reality that He actively manifests?
?
The passage argues that existence alone does not make something an agent. You believe God exists, but do you functionally treat Him as an agent in your daily decisions, or do you act as if He is a passive observer? Where in your life have you been content with a 'God exists' mentality without letting that lead you to acknowledge His active attributes at work right now?
?
Al-Ka'bi's error was reducing divine evidence to essence only. Have you ever reduced your understanding of God to a bare concept—something you affirm with your mind but fail to engage with as a being of infinite attributes? If so, what is one attribute you have neglected, and what would it look like to let that attribute shape your next hour?

beginner

Abu Hashim's State Theory Implies Knowledge
First part of refutation showing state theory implies knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hashim's claim that a well-made act indicates a state necessarily requires the existence of knowledge and power in the agent.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim's state theory concedes the necessity of knowledge.

Abu Hashim al-Basri argued that the agent's capability is due to a special 'state' (hal) indicated by the well-made act. The author begins the refutation by demonstrating that this claim logically entails the existence of power and knowledge, because in the witnessed world such a state is only possible through knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on understanding the argument that Abu Hashim's own statement implies the sangat of power and knowledge.
2
Understand the analogy between witnessed and unseen
Notice how the author uses the principle of analogy between the witnessed world (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib) to argue that if a state in the witnessed requires knowledge, the same must hold for the unseen.
This is a key reasoning tool in kalam.
3
Identify the conclusion
The author concludes that the well-made act becomes evidence for the state, and the state becomes evidence for 'the sciences' (i.e., knowledge). Ask yourself: why does this refute Abu Hashim?
Abu Hashim wanted to avoid attributing separate knowledge to God, but his theory inadvertently requires it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لأبي هاشم إن قولك: إن الفعل المحكم دلالة على أن الفاعل له حال لكونه عليها كان قادرا إقرار منك بثبوت القدرة وكذا العلم، لأن الفعل إذا دل على تلك الحال وهي في الشاهد من مقتضيات العلم فلا يمكن ثبوتها في الشاهد إلا بالعلم فكذا في الغائب تسوية بين الشاهد والغائب في تعلق المتلازمات بعضها بعض فصار المحكم المتقن دليل الحال، والحال دليل العلوم. ثم يقال له: إذا كان الفعل المحكم المتقن دليل حال لكون الفاعل عليها كان عالما، وكانت الحال مدلولا عليها من جهة الفعل المحكم المتقن، فهل الحال معلومة أم لا ؟ فإن قلت: إنها معلومة فقد تركت مذهبك، وإن قلت إنها ليست بمعلومة فقد هذيت، فإن الحال لما كانت مدلولا عليها، وقد ظهر ما هو دليل عليها لابد من أن تصير معلومة، ولو جاز أن لا يصير المدلول معلوما مع المعرفة بالدليل، لجاز أن لا يصير ذات الفاعل معلوما مع وجود الدليل والعلم بالله تعالى استدلالي، فإذا لم يكن أبو هاشم عالما بالله تعالى. ولأن الدليل ما يوجد منه الدلالة لو استدل المستدل ثم يقال له : هل دل الفعل المحكم على الحال أم لا؟ فإن قال : لا ، فقد أقر أنه ليس بدليل، فدعواه أنه دليل الحال ضرب من المحال وإن قال : دل قيل له : إذا وجدت الدلالة واستدل المستدل واستوفى شرائط الاستدلال، كيف لم يصر المدلول معلوما؟ ويقال له: إذا كان الفعل المحكم المتقن يدل على الحال والحال لم تصر معلومة كيف عرفت أن الفاعل عالم وكونه عالما من مقتضيات الحال والعلم بالمقتضى يثبت بثبوت العلم بالمقتضى.. ثم يقال له : إذا كان الفعل المحكم المتقن يدل على الحال فالحال راجعة إلى عين الذات أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن قلت : إنها راجعة إلى عين الذات فقد التحقت بأبيك، وقد أبطلنا ذلك، وإن قلت هي معنى وراء الذات، فقد أقررت بما منه هربت أنك أخطأت في تسمية ذلك حالا وبالله التوفيق
And it is said to Abu Hashim: Your statement that the well-made act indicates that the agent has a state (hal) by virtue of which he is capable is an acknowledgment from you of the establishment of power and likewise knowledge, because if the act indicates that state, and it in the witnessed is among the requirements of knowledge, then its establishment in the witnessed is only through knowledge. So likewise in the unseen, by analogy between the witnessed and the unseen regarding the interconnection of correlatives. Thus the perfectly well-made act becomes evidence of the state, and the state evidence of the sciences. Then it is said to him: If the perfectly well-made act is evidence of a state by virtue of which the agent is knowing, and the state is indicated by the perfectly well-made act, then is the state known or not? If you say it is known, you have abandoned your school. If you say it is not known, you have raved. For since the state is indicated and what is evidence for it has appeared, it must become known. And if it were possible that the indicated not become known despite knowledge of the evidence, then it would be possible that the essence of the agent not become known despite the existence of evidence, and knowledge of Allah the Exalted is inferential; then Abu Hashim would not be knowledgeable of Allah the Exalted. Also because evidence is that from which indication comes if the inquirer inquires. Then it is said to him: Did the well-made act indicate the state or not? If he says no, then he has acknowledged that it is not evidence, so his claim that it is evidence of the state is absurd. If he says yes, it is said to him: When the indication occurred and the inquirer inquired and fulfilled the conditions of inquiry, why did the indicated not become known? And it is said to him: If the perfectly well-made act indicates the state, and the state has not become known, how did you know that the agent is knowing? And his being knowing is among the requirements of the state, and knowledge of the requirement is established by establishment of knowledge of the requirement. Then it is said to him: If the perfectly well-made act indicates the state, then is the state referable to the essence itself or to a meaning beyond the essence? If you say it is referable to the essence itself, then you have joined your father (al-Ka'bi), and we have already invalidated that. If you say it is a meaning beyond the essence, then you have acknowledged what you were fleeing from: that you erred in calling that a state. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of Abu Hashim's theory of 'state' (hal). The author begins by pointing out that Abu Hashim's claim that the well-made act indicates a state which makes the agent capable implies an admission of the existence of power and knowledge. Because in the witnessed world, such a state is a consequence of knowledge, so its establishment requires knowledge. The same should hold in the unseen by analogy. Then the author poses a dilemma: If the state is indicated by the act, is it known or not? If it is known, then Abu Hashim abandons his position (states are supposed to be neither existent nor non-existent and thus not known). If it is not known, that is absurd because the indication should lead to knowledge. If the indicated can remain unknown despite the evidence, then the essence itself could remain unknown, making inferential knowledge of God impossible. Further, if the act does not indicate the state, it is not evidence; if it does, then why is the state not known? The author also questions how Abu Hashim can claim knowledge of the agent's being knowing if the state remains unknown. Finally, the same dilemma about essence is applied: if the state is identical to essence, he joins al-Ka'bi and is refuted; if it is a distinct meaning, he admits his error in calling it a state. The passage ends with 'with Allah is success'.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does Abu Hashim's statement about the well-made act actually admit?
A) That the agent has a state
B) That the agent has power and knowledge
C) That the act is well-made
D) That the state is unknown

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's own theory forces him to concede that knowledge is required for the agent's state, contradicting his denial of distinct divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the perfect act reveals God's knowledge, then where in your daily life do you look for signs of that knowledge? Do you truly see His hand in the order of creation, or do you attribute it to blind chance? What would it mean for your life if God's knowledge of every detail is certain?
?
Abu Hashim's theory tried to affirm God's action while denying distinct attributes. In your own heart, have you ever tried to affirm God's existence while avoiding the full implications of His knowledge of your secret thoughts and deeds? Are you willing to live with the reality that God knows everything about you, even what you try to hide?
?
The argument forces a logical conclusion: if you see a well-made act, you must admit the agent's knowledge. Look at your own actions—what do they say about the knowledge you attribute to God? If your life is disordered, does that reflect a lack of trust in His knowledge? Commit today to seeing every detail of your life as under His knowing gaze.

The Epistemic and Ontological Refutation of States
Second part of refutation with dilemmas of knowledge and essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two dilemmas that destroy Abu Hashim's theory: the state must be either known or not (epistemic), and either identical to essence or a separate meaning (ontological).

Core Idea
The state is either known or not; either essence or not—each option refutes Abu Hashim.

The author continues the refutation with a sharp dilemma. If the state indicated by the act is known, Abu Hashim abandons his own school. If it is not known, then inferential knowledge of God becomes impossible. Further, the author questions whether the state is the essence itself or a separate meaning—both options lead to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first dilemma: known or not known
Read the passage carefully to see how the author forces a choice: either the state is known or it is not. Consider the consequences of each option.
Abu Hashim believed states are neither existent nor non-existent and thus not knowable in the usual sense.
2
Understand the implication for knowledge of God
Notice the argument that if the indicated (state) can remain unknown despite evidence, then the essence of the agent could also remain unknown, which would destroy inferential knowledge of God.
This shows the theory leads to atheism.
3
Analyze the second dilemma: essence or distinct meaning
The author then asks whether the state is identical to the essence or a separate meaning. If identical, Abu Hashim joins al-Ka'bi and is refuted. If separate, he admits his mistake.
This completes the refutation by showing the theory is incoherent ontologically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Examine your own faith: is it built on evidence that necessarily leads to knowledge, or do you allow for uncertainty where the evidence should leave no room?
?
The state of being knowing is a requirement for the agent. If you claim to know that God is knowing, but you cannot explain what that knowingness is (state or attribute), are you really certain of what you believe? What would it mean for your worship if the foundation of God's attributes were uncertain?
?
Abu Hashim tried to avoid both identifying states with essence and making them separate attributes. Have you ever tried to hold a middle ground that avoids clear categories, only to find it logically unstable? Where in your own understanding of God are you avoiding a clear choice between two consistent positions? Commit to not settling for ambiguity.

Objection by al-Ka'bi's Followers: Necessary vs Acquired Knowledge
Objection and partial response regarding necessary vs acquired knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that knowledge of God's being knowing is intuitive, but knowledge of His attribute of knowledge is acquired, and the beginning of the response regarding differentiation between the witnessed and unseen.

Core Idea
The objection distinguishes intuitive knowledge of God from acquired knowledge of His attributes.

This passage presents a key objection from the followers of al-Ka'bi to the argument from the witnessed to the unseen regarding divine attributes. They argue that while knowledge that the agent (God) is knowing is intuitive and necessary, the knowledge that He possesses the attribute of knowledge is acquired through inference, because it involves unseen accidents. The author begins to respond by denying any basis for differentiation between the seen and unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how al-Ka'bi's followers distinguish between two kinds of knowledge: necessary (intuitive) and acquired (inferential).
Pay attention to the terms 'ضروري' and 'مكتسب'.
2
Identify the two claims
They claim that knowing the agent is knowing is necessary, but knowing that He has knowledge is acquired.
This sets up the problem for the argument from the witnessed.
3
Understand the beginning of the response
The author says there is no reason to differentiate between the two realms, then points out that the evidence for knowledge in the witnessed (the possibility of ignorance) is absent in the unseen.
The response is incomplete; consider what might complete it.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage cuts off. Reason that since ignorance is impossible for God, the inference from possibility of ignorance does not apply to the unseen.
This is a common tactic in kalam: denying the applicability of a condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض أصحاب الكعبي على هذا الدليل، وقالوا: إن العلم بكون الفاعل عالما ضروري ثابت بالبديهة، فحكمنا به في الشاهد والغائب. فأما العلم بثبوت العلم له في الشاهد مكتسب لأن العلم بالأعراض الغائبة عن الحواس لن يكون إلا بطريق الاستدلال والاكتساب، والعلم الثابت بالبديهة غير الثابت بالاستدلال . فقلنا : يكون الفاعل عالما في الشاهد والغائب جميعا، لانعدام الموجب للتفرقة بينهما ، فأما دليل ثبوت العلم في الشاهد وهو جواز كونه جاهلا غير عالم فمنعدم في الغائب، إذ التعدية من الشاهد إلى
And the followers of al-Ka'bi objected to this evidence and said: The knowledge that the agent is knowing is necessary, established by intuition, so we judge by it in both the witnessed and the unseen. As for the knowledge of the establishment of knowledge for Him in the witnessed, it is acquired because knowledge of accidents absent from the senses is only by way of inference and acquisition, and knowledge established by intuition is not the same as that established by inference. So we said: The agent is knowing in both the witnessed and the unseen, due to the lack of any factor necessitating differentiation between them. As for the evidence for the establishment of knowledge in the witnessed, which is the possibility of Him being ignorant and not knowing, it is absent in the unseen, for the transfer from the witnessed to the [unseen] ...
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the followers of al-Ka'bi and the beginning of a response. The objectors argue that the knowledge that the agent is knowing is necessary (daruri) and intuitive (badihi), so it applies equally to the witnessed (shahid) and the unseen (gha'ib). However, the knowledge that the agent possesses knowledge (i.e., the attribute of knowledge) in the witnessed is acquired (muktasab) because it involves knowledge of accidents, which requires inference. Intuitive knowledge and inferential knowledge are different. The response (beginning with 'fa qulna') states that the agent is knowing in both realms because there is no reason to differentiate. But the evidence for the establishment of knowledge in the witnessed—namely, the possibility that the agent could be ignorant—is absent in the unseen. The argument is cut off, but the idea is that the possibility of ignorance, which serves as evidence for knowledge in the witnessed, does not apply to God in the unseen because ignorance is impossible for Him. The passage ends incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that intuitive knowledge of God's attributes is sufficient and that no inference is needed.
Correction: The objectors argue that knowledge of God's attributes (like His knowledge) is not intuitive but acquired through inference. The passage highlights the need to distinguish between different types of knowledge and the methods by which they are established.

Checkpoint
According to the objectors, what is the status of the knowledge that the agent is knowing?
A) It is acquired through inference.
B) It is necessary and intuitive.
C) It is only valid in the witnessed.
D) It is based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
The objection distinguishes between intuitive knowledge of God's existence/agency and acquired knowledge of His attributes, but the response denies a basis for differentiating the two realms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your knowledge of God's attributes as a mere mental exercise or as a living reality that demands your trust? How would your life change if you truly lived as if God's knowledge of you is as real as your own pulse?
?
The objectors relied on inference to know God's attributes. When you face trials, do you fall back on reasoning or on intuitive certainty? Which one sustains you in moments of doubt?
?
If you know intuitively that God knows you, why do you still act as if you need to prove yourself to Him? What would it look like to surrender that need and rest in His knowledge?

Refutation of Abu Hashim's Theory of the State (Hal)
Refutation through logical dilemmas and conditional consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of Abu Hashim's theory of the state (hal) and why it leads to contradictions.

Core Idea
The state cannot be an intermediate entity; it is either the essence or an attribute.

In this passage, the author presents a systematic refutation of Abu Hashim's theory of the state (hal) by exposing its logical flaws. He argues that if a state is indicated by an act, it must become known; otherwise, the entire inferential structure for knowing Allah collapses. Through a series of dilemmas, he forces Abu Hashim into contradictions, demonstrating that the concept of a state as a distinct entity is untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the conditional statements and the dilemmas.
2
Identify the core dilemma
The author presents a dilemma: either the precise act indicates the state or it does not. Follow each branch.
If it does not, the claim that it is evidence is false. If it does, the state must become known.
3
Examine the implications of the state being unknown
The author argues that if the state remains unknown despite evidence, then by analogy the essence of the agent could also remain unknown, undermining all inferential knowledge of Allah.
4
Analyze the final dilemma on the nature of the state
The author asks whether the state is identical to the essence or a distinct meaning. Either way, the theory collapses.
If identical, it reduces to Abu Ali's view (already refuted). If distinct, Abu Hashim admitted he misnamed it (it is actually an attribute).
5
Reflect on the argument's structure
Consider how the author uses logical necessity and dilemmas to refute the opponent without asserting a positive doctrine.
This is a classic kalam method of ilzam (forcing a contradiction).

تركت مذهبك، وإن قلت إنها ليست بمعلومة فقد هذيت، فإن الحال لما كانت مدلولا عليها، وقد ظهر ما هو دليل عليها لابد من أن تصير معلومة، ولو جاز أن لا يصير المدلول معلوما مع المعرفة بالدليل، لجاز أن لا يصير ذات الفاعل معلوما مع وجود الدليل والعلم بالله تعالى استدلالي، فإذا لم يكن أبو هاشم عالما بالله تعالى. ولأن الدليل ما يوجد منه الدلالة لو استدل المستدل ثم يقال له : هل دل الفعل المحكم على الحال أم لا؟ فإن قال : لا ، فقد أقر أنه ليس بدليل، فدعواه أنه دليل الحال ضرب من المحال وإن قال : دل قيل له : إذا وجدت الدلالة واستدل المستدل واستوفى شرائط الاستدلال، كيف لم يصر المدلول معلوما؟ ويقال له: إذا كان الفعل المحكم المتقن يدل على الحال والحال لم تصر معلومة كيف عرفت أن الفاعل عالم وكونه عالما من مقتضيات الحال والعلم بالمقتضى يثبت بثبوت العلم بالمقتضى.. ثم يقال له : إذا كان الفعل المحكم المتقن يدل على الحال فالحال راجعة إلى عين الذات أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن قلت : إنها راجعة إلى عين الذات فقد التحقت بأبيك، وقد أبطلنا ذلك، وإن قلت هي معنى وراء الذات، فقد أقررت بما منه هربت أنك أخطأت في تسمية ذلك حالا وبالله التوفيق
You have abandoned your school. If you say it (the state) is not known, then you have raved. For if the state is indicated, and its evidence has appeared, it must become known. If it were permissible that the indicated not become known despite knowledge of the evidence, then it would be permissible that the essence of the agent not become known despite the existence of evidence. And knowledge of Allah is inferential. Thus if Abu Hashim is not knowing of Allah. And because the evidence is that from which indication comes when the inquirer inquires. Then it is said to him: Does the precise act indicate the state or not? If he says: No, then he has admitted that it is not evidence. So his claim that it is evidence of the state is impossible. If he says: It does indicate, it is said to him: When the indication is found, the inquirer inquires, and he fulfills the conditions of inquiry, how does the indicated not become known? And it is said to him: If the precise, perfect act indicates the state, and the state has not become known, how did you know that the agent is knowing? And his being knowing is one of the requisites of the state, and knowledge of the requisite is established by the establishment of knowledge of the state. Then it is said to him: If the precise, perfect act indicates the state, does the state refer back to the essence itself or to a meaning beyond the essence? If you say it refers back to the essence itself, you have adhered to your father, and we have refuted that. If you say it is a meaning beyond the essence, then you have admitted what you were fleeing from: that you erred in calling that a state. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Abu Hashim's theory of the state (hal). The author begins by stating that if Abu Hashim denies that the state is known, then he is deluded. The argument proceeds as follows: If the state is indicated by the act, then it must become known when the evidence is present. Otherwise, the same logic would allow that the existence of the agent might remain unknown despite evidence, which would undermine inferential knowledge of Allah. The author then poses a dilemma to Abu Hashim: either the act indicates the state or not. If not, then it is not evidence, and his claim is impossible. If it does indicate, then why is the state not known when all conditions are met? The author argues that since the act indicates the state, and the state's knowledge is necessary to know that the agent is knowing, if the state is unknown, then how can one claim to know the agent is knowing? Finally, the author asks whether the state is identical to the essence or a distinct meaning. If identical, then the position collapses to that of his father (Abu Ali). If distinct, then Abu Hashim has conceded that he erred in calling it a state, since it is actually an attribute. The passage ends with 'And with Allah is success.'

Key Terms
حال (hal)
مدلول (indicated)
دليل (evidence)
فعل محكم (precise act)
ذات (essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The state (hal) is an intermediate entity between essence and attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent.
Correction: The author shows that the state must either be identical to the essence or a distinct attribute. There is no third option.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would follow if the state (hal) were not known despite the presence of its evidence?
A) The state would still be knowable through other means
B) The agent's essence could also remain unknown despite evidence
C) The state would be identical to the act
D) The argument would be invalid

Key Takeaway
The state (hal) cannot be a third entity; it is either the essence or a distinct attribute, and any theory positing otherwise leads to logical contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If knowledge of Allah is inferential, upon what evidence do you base your belief in Him? Have you ever considered that neglecting to follow correct inference might lead you to ignorance of Allah's attributes?
?
The author argues that if evidence points to a truth, that truth must become known to the sincere seeker. In your own spiritual journey, are there clear signs from Allah that you have willfully ignored? What 'states' of your heart are you failing to acknowledge despite overwhelming evidence?
?
The passage ends with 'And with Allah is success.' Do you truly rely on Allah for guidance in theological matters, or do you trust your own rationality alone? How can this lesson humble you in your approach to understanding Allah's nature?

The Acquired Nature of Knowing the Agent: Refuting the Ka'bi School
Refutation of the claim that knowledge of the agent's being knowing is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge that the agent is knowing is acquired, not intuitive, and how the argument distinguishes between two types of indications.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the agent's being knowing is built on acquired knowledge, so it must be acquired.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection from the Ka'bi school of Mu'tazila who claim that our knowledge that the agent (Allah) is knowing is necessary and intuitive. The author systematically refutes this, showing that such knowledge depends on prior acquired knowledge of the agent from the act, and thus cannot be intuitive. He also clarifies the difference between the indication for the agent being knowing and the indication for the existence of knowledge in the agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the first part of the passage where the Ka'bi school presents their claim. Notice how the author responds by stating there is no cause for differentiation between witnessed and unseen.
The Ka'bi claim relies on a distinction between necessary and acquired knowledge.
2
Identify the two indications
The author then introduces a distinction between the indication for the agent being knowing and the indication for the existence of knowledge. Find where this is explained.
The distinction is supported by the possibility of knowing one without the other.
3
Follow the evidence from the deniers of attributes
The author cites the deniers of attributes (nufat al-a'rad) as an example: they know the agent is knowing but deny the existence of knowledge as an attribute. See how this proves the separation.
4
Understand the final refutation
The author concludes by showing that knowledge of the agent being knowing depends on acquired knowledge of the agent from the act, so it cannot be intuitive. Read the final argument carefully.
The key is that the foundation (agent) is known by acquisition, so what is built on it must also be acquired.

واعترض أصحاب الكعبي على هذا الدليل، وقالوا: إن العلم بكون الفاعل عالما ضروري ثابت بالبديهة، فحكمنا به في الشاهد والغائب. فأما العلم بثبوت العلم له في الشاهد مكتسب لأن العلم بالأعراض الغائبة عن الحواس لن يكون إلا بطريق الاستدلال والاكتساب، والعلم الثابت بالبديهة غير الثابت بالاستدلال . فقلنا : يكون الفاعل عالما في الشاهد والغائب جميعا، لانعدام الموجب للتفرقة بينهما ، فأما دليل ثبوت العلم في الشاهد وهو جواز كونه جاهلا غير عالم فمنعدم في الغائب، إذ التعدية من الشاهد إلى الغائب تجب عند قيام دليل التعدية، ووجود المعنى الموجب للتسوية ولم يوجد. وعامة المعتزلة يسلمون أن العلم بكون الفاعل عالما مكتسب كالعلم بثبوت العلم للفاعل غير أن الدلالة التي تدل على كونه عالما في الشاهد غير الدلالة على ثبوت العلم، بدليل أن العلم بأحدهما ينفك عن الآخر، ولو كانت هذه الدلالة عين تلك الدلالة لما تصور الانفكاك بينهما، لأن الشيء الواحد لا يتصور أن يدل على مدلول له ولا يدل على المدلول الآخر ، إذ هو يدل على الأمرين بوجوده وقد وجد، وإذا كان كذلك فكانت الدلالة على كون الفاعل عالما موجودة في الشاهد والغائب جميعا، فحكم بكونه عالما . فأما الدليل على ثبوت العلم له فموجود في الشاهد دون الغائب وهو ما ذكرنا من جواز كونه غير عالم ففرقنا بينهما ودليل جواز الانفكاك بين الدليلين أن نفاة الأعراض يعلمون كون الفاعل عالما ولا يعلمون ثبوت العلم له، فيقال لأصحاب الكعبي : إن دعوى كون العلم بكون الفاعل عالما من باب البديهيات دعوى ممنوعة بل هي من جملة المستحيلات، فإن العلم بكونه عالما منبئ على العلم بكونه فاعلا، والعلم بالفاعل بدلالة الفعل المحكم على مكتسب بمساعدة الكعبي إيانا إذ المعارف ليست بضرورية، وإذا كان العالم بثبوت الفاعل للمفعول مكتسبا، كيف يكون العلم بما هو مبني عليه ضروريا بديهيا فإن من زعم أن العلم المبني على العلم المكتسب ضروري - وإن كان لا يتوصل إليه إلا بالتوصل إلى الأول، ولن يتوصل إلى الأول إلا بالاستدلال وصار هو بهذه الواسطة مستحيل الحصول إلا بالاكتساب والاستدلال - كان متحامقا ، وهذا لأنه جعل المعرفة لمن عاين المفعول المحكم المتقن بكون فاعله عالما قادرا ثابتة بطريق الضرورة حال ما
The followers of al-Ka'bi objected to this proof, saying: Knowledge that the agent is knowing is necessary, established by intuition. So we judge by it in the witnessed and the unseen. As for knowledge of the existence of knowledge for Him in the witnessed, it is acquired, because knowledge of attributes absent from the senses cannot be except by way of inference and acquisition. And knowledge established by intuition is other than that established by inference. So we said: The agent is knowing in both the witnessed and the unseen, due to the absence of any cause necessitating differentiation between them. As for the evidence for the existence of knowledge in the witnessed, which is the possibility of his being ignorant and not knowing, it is absent in the unseen, since transference from the witnessed to the unseen is necessary when evidence for transference exists, and the meaning necessitating equality exists and has not been found. And the general body of the Mu'tazila concede that knowledge that the agent is knowing is acquired like the knowledge of the existence of knowledge for the agent, except that the indication that indicates his being knowing in the witnessed is different from the indication for the existence of knowledge, as evidenced by the fact that knowledge of one of them can be separated from the other. If this indication were the same as that indication, separation between them would be unimaginable, because a single thing cannot be conceived to indicate one of its two indicated objects without indicating the other, since it indicates both by its existence and it has existed. And if this is so, then the indication for the agent's being knowing exists in both the witnessed and the unseen, so we judge that He is knowing. As for the evidence for the existence of knowledge for Him, it exists in the witnessed but not in the unseen, and it is what we mentioned: the possibility of his being not knowing. So we differentiated between them. And the evidence for the possibility of separation between the two indications is that the deniers of attributes know that the agent is knowing but do not know the existence of knowledge for Him. So it is said to the followers of al-Ka'bi: The claim that knowledge that the agent is knowing belongs to the category of intuitions is a prohibited claim; rather it is among the impossibilities. For knowledge that He is knowing informs about knowledge that He is an agent, and knowledge of the agent by way of the indication of the precise act is acquired, with the assistance of al-Ka'bi to us, since cognitions are not necessary. And if knowledge of the existence of the agent for the effect is acquired, how can knowledge that is built upon it be necessary and intuitive? For whoever claims that knowledge built upon acquired knowledge is necessary—even if it cannot be attained except by reaching the first, and the first cannot be attained except by inference, and thus by this mediation it becomes impossible to obtain except by acquisition and inference—he is foolish. This is because he made the knowledge, for one who observes the precise, perfect effect, that its agent is knowing and powerful, established by way of necessity while...
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Ka'bi school and the author's response. The Ka'bi school argues that knowledge that the agent is knowing is necessary (daruri) and intuitive (badihi), while knowledge of the existence of knowledge (ilm) for the agent is acquired (muktasab). In the witnessed world, we have both, but in the unseen (al-gha'ib) only the necessary knowledge applies. The author first responds by stating that the agent is knowing in both realms because there is no cause for differentiation. The evidence for the existence of knowledge (the possibility of ignorance) is limited to the witnessed, so transference is not required. The author then notes that the general Mu'tazila agree that knowledge that the agent is knowing is acquired, but they distinguish between two indications: one for the agent being knowing and another for the existence of knowledge. These are distinct because one can be known without the other. The indication for being knowing exists universally, while the indication for existence of knowledge is limited to the witnessed. The author then delivers a final refutation to the Ka'bi school: claiming that knowledge of the agent being knowing is intuitive is impossible because it depends on knowledge of the agent, which is itself acquired from the act. Thus it is built on acquired knowledge and must be acquired. To claim it is necessary is contradictory and foolish.

بديهة (intuition)
مكتسب (acquired)
شاهد (witnessed)
غائب (unseen)
تعدية (transference)
دلالة (indication)
انفكاك (separation)
نفاة الأعراض (deniers of attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge that the agent is knowing is intuitive/necessary and does not require inference.
Correction: This knowledge depends on acquiring knowledge of the agent from the act, and is therefore acquired, not intuitive.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the problem with the Ka'bi claim that knowledge of the agent being knowing is intuitive?
A) It would make the act unnecessary
B) It is built upon acquired knowledge of the agent, so it must be acquired
C) The Ka'bi school denied the distinction between witnessed and unseen
D) It would require the state (hal) theory

Key Takeaway
Knowledge that the agent is knowing is not intuitive; it is an acquired knowledge built on the prior acquired knowledge of the agent from the act.

?

Two Types of Evidence for Divine Knowledge
Distinguishing two types of evidence for divine knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the evidence that an agent is knowing and the evidence that the agent possesses knowledge, and why they are separate in kalam reasoning.

Core Idea
Knowing that someone is knowing is not the same as knowing they have knowledge.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle but crucial distinction in the theology of divine attributes. Before we can attribute knowledge to Allah, we must clarify what kind of evidence we have. The Mu'tazila agree that knowledge of an agent being knowing is acquired, but they fail to see that the evidence for 'being knowing' differs from the evidence for 'possessing knowledge'. This passage explains why these two pieces of evidence can and do separate, using the example of those who deny the reality of accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's use of 'shahid' and 'gha'ib' and the key terms 'dalala' and 'muktasab'.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two pieces of evidence
Find where the author contrasts the evidence for 'being knowing' (كون الفاعل عالما) and the evidence for 'possession of knowledge' (ثبوت العلم).
These are the two types of dalala.
3
Understand the proof of separation
The author argues that because one can know one without the other, they must be different. Look for the example of 'nufat al-a'rad' (deniers of accidents).
This is a concrete counterexample.
4
Apply the distinction to the unseen
The author concludes that evidence for being knowing exists in both shahid and gha'ib, but evidence for possession of knowledge exists only in shahid. Reflect on why this matters for attributing knowledge to Allah.
This is the key theological conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغائب تجب عند قيام دليل التعدية، ووجود المعنى الموجب للتسوية ولم يوجد. وعامة المعتزلة يسلمون أن العلم بكون الفاعل عالما مكتسب كالعلم بثبوت العلم للفاعل غير أن الدلالة التي تدل على كونه عالما في الشاهد غير الدلالة على ثبوت العلم، بدليل أن العلم بأحدهما ينفك عن الآخر، ولو كانت هذه الدلالة عين تلك الدلالة لما تصور الانفكاك بينهما، لأن الشيء الواحد لا يتصور أن يدل على مدلول له ولا يدل على المدلول الآخر، إذ هو يدل على الأمرين بوجوده وقد وجد، وإذا كان كذلك فكانت الدلالة على كون الفاعل عالما موجودة في الشاهد والغائب جميعا، فحكم بكونه عالما. فأما الدليل على ثبوت العلم له فموجود في الشاهد دون الغائب وهو ما ذكرنا من جواز كونه غير عالم ففرقنا بينهما ودليل جواز الانفكاك بين الدليلين أن نفاة الأعراض يعلمون كون الفاعل عالما ولا يعلمون ثبوت العلم له.
The unseen becomes necessary when evidence of derivation exists, and the meaning necessitating equality exists but does not exist. And the majority of the Mu'tazila admit that knowledge of the agent being knowing is acquired, like knowledge of the possession of knowledge for the agent, except that the evidence which indicates that he is knowing in the witnessed is not the same as the evidence for the possession of knowledge, because knowledge of one can separate from the other. If this evidence were that same evidence, separation between them would not be conceivable, because a single thing cannot be imagined to indicate one of its implications and not indicate the other, since it indicates both by its existence and it has existed. And if that is so, then the evidence for the agent being knowing exists in both the witnessed and the unseen, so it is judged that he is knowing. As for the evidence for the possession of knowledge for him, it exists in the witnessed without the unseen, which is what we mentioned: the possibility of him not being knowing. So we distinguished between them, and the proof of the possibility of separation between the two pieces of evidence is that the deniers of accidents know that the agent is knowing but do not know the possession of knowledge for him.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two distinct pieces of evidence: the evidence (dalala) that an agent is knowing ('alim) and the evidence that the agent possesses knowledge ('ilm). The author begins by stating a general rule about the unseen (gha'ib) being necessary when there is a proof of transfer (ta'diya) and the meaning requiring equivalence. He then notes that the Mu'tazila generally agree that knowledge of the agent being knowing is acquired (muktasab) just like knowledge of the possession of knowledge. However, he points out that the evidence for the agent being knowing in the witnessed (shahid) is not the same as the evidence for possession of knowledge. The proof (daleel) is that one can know the former without knowing the latter. If they were identical, separation would be impossible. Since they are separable, the pieces of evidence are distinct. Furthermore, the evidence for the agent being knowing is present in both the shahid and the gha'ib, so we can judge that the agent (e.g., God) is knowing. The evidence for possession of knowledge is only in the shahid because in the gha'ib we cannot directly know the attribute of knowledge; we only know the agent is knowing. The proof that they can be separated is that the deniers of accidents (nufat al-a'rad), who deny the existence of accidents, know that the agent is knowing but do not know that he possesses knowledge. This shows the two pieces of evidence are distinct.

Key Terms
دلالة
الشاهد
الغائب
المعتزلة
المكتسب
نفاة الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that if the agent is known to be knowing, that automatically means the agent has knowledge (i.e., the two pieces of evidence are the same).
Correction: The author demonstrates that the evidence for being knowing is distinct from the evidence for having knowledge, as shown by the deniers of accidents who affirm the former but not the latter.

Checkpoint
What proof does the author give that the evidence for the agent being knowing is distinct from the evidence for the possession of knowledge?
A) The Mu'tazila admit it.
B) Knowledge of one can separate from the other, and deniers of accidents know the agent is knowing but not that he has knowledge.
C) The agent is the same in both worlds.
D) The evidence is found in both worlds.

Key Takeaway
The evidence that an agent is knowing (e.g., God) is present in both the witnessed and the unseen, but the evidence that the agent possesses knowledge is only in the witnessed; they are two distinct pieces of evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that knowing God is knowing automatically means you understand His attribute of knowledge fully? This lesson shows they require separate evidence. Where in your own faith have you conflated two distinct truths about God, and how will you seek to distinguish them from now on?
?
The deniers of accidents could still affirm that God is knowing without affirming possession of knowledge. Are there aspects of God you affirm but without having truly worked through the evidence from revelation and reason? What will you do this week to examine one such belief more carefully?
?
This argument forces you to admit that some evidence about God is only available in the created world (shāhid). In what area of your life are you relying on worldly evidence alone to form a view of God, while neglecting what the unseen (ghā'ib) requires? Commit now to a specific action that will realign your understanding.

The Act Indicates Knowledge and Knower as One
Author argues against Mu'tazila that the act indicates both knowledge and knower as one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Māturīdī position holds that the act indicates both the attribute of knowledge and the agent's being knowing as a single indication, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilī view.

Core Idea
Knowing and having knowledge are two sides of the same coin; the act shows both.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafī challenges the Mu'tazila who differentiate between the act's indication that its agent is knowing and its indication that He has knowledge. By forcing them into a logical division, he exposes the incoherence of their position and affirms that the divine attributes are real and distinct from the essence. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dialectical style: the author asks questions and presents divisions.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilī claim
The Mu'tazila claim that the act gives two separate indications: one that the agent is knowing, and another that he has knowledge. Identify this claim in the opening question.
This is the target of the refutation.
3
Follow the logical division
The author presents a division: does 'being knowing' refer to the essence alone, or to an essence described by an attribute? Then within the latter, does the attribute revert to the essence or is it beyond? See how each branch is handled.
The first branch is refuted, the second leads to the truth.
4
See the refutation of separation
The author argues that if the Mu'tazila accept the final branch (attribute beyond essence), then the indication of knowing is identical to the indication of having knowledge. Their attempted separation is invalid.
This is the core of the argument.
5
Note the additional argument about accidents
The author adds that deniers of accidents (like the Mu'tazila who deny attributes) cannot even know an agent in the sensible world, because they reject acts as accidents. This further undermines their position.
This reinforces the main argument.

ويقال لعامة المعتزلة: لم تقولون: إن دلالة الفعل على كون فاعله عالما غير الدلالة على ثبوت علمه؟ وما معنى كونه عالما؟ أراجع هو إلى الذات فحسب، أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن قلتم: إنه راجع إلى الذات فحسب فقد مر إبطاله. وإن قلتم: إنه راجع إلى ذات موصوف بصفة. قلنا: التقسيم قائم، فإنا نقول: يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى عين الذات أو على ذات موصوف بصفة وراء الذات؟ فإن قلتم: يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى عين الذات فقد مر إبطاله. وإن قلتم يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى معنى وراء الذات، فقد أقررتم بالحق. وتبين أن الدلالة على كونه عالما هي الدلالة على أن له علما، وما ذكر من الانفكاك ممنوع، وما ذكر من الدليل باطل، فإنا لا نسلم أن نفاة الأعراض يعرفون في الشاهد فاعلا، فإن المعرفة بالفاعل لن تتصور بدون المعرفة بالفعل، وهم لا يعرفون في الشاهد فعلا ليعرفوا به فاعلا وهذا لأن الفاعل في الشاهد لا يفعل الجسم إنما يفعل العرض، وهؤلاء ينكرون الأعراض فكيف يعرفون الفعل، وإذا لم يعرفوا الفعل لا يعرفوا الفاعل على أنا أقمنا الدلالة على أن الفعل المحكم يدل على قدرة الفاعل وعلمه، فبعد ذلك لا حاجة بنا إلى أن نبين أن الدلالة على العلم والعالم دلالة واحدة أو دلالتان. والله الموفق.
And it is said to the generality of the Mu'tazila: Why do you say that the indication of the act that its agent is knowing is other than the indication of the existence of His knowledge? And what is the meaning of His being knowing? Does it refer back to the essence alone, or to a meaning beyond the essence? If you say it refers back to the essence alone, its refutation has already passed. If you say it refers back to an essence described by an attribute, we say: the division stands. For we say: does it indicate an essence described by an attribute that reverts to the very essence, or an essence described by an attribute beyond the essence? If you say it indicates an essence described by an attribute that reverts to the very essence, its refutation has already passed. If you say it indicates an essence described by an attribute reverting to a meaning beyond the essence, then you have acknowledged the truth. And it becomes clear that the indication of His being knowing is the indication that He has knowledge. And what is mentioned of separation is prohibited, and what is mentioned of the proof is false. For we do not concede that deniers of accidents know an agent in the witnessed world. Indeed, knowledge of the agent is inconceivable without knowledge of the act, and they do not know an act in the witnessed world by which they could know an agent. This is because the agent in the witnessed world does not produce the body, but only produces the accident. And these people deny accidents, so how can they know the act? And if they do not know the act, they do not know the agent. Moreover, we have established the proof that the well-ordered act indicates the power and knowledge of the agent. After that, we have no need to show whether the indication of knowledge and the knower is one indication or two indications. And Allah is the Guide.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an argument against the Mu'tazila who claim that the act's indication that the agent is knowing (ʿālim) is different from its indication that He has knowledge (ʿilm). The author challenges them to clarify what 'being knowing' means. If it refers to the essence alone, that is refuted because it would collapse the attribute into the essence. If it refers to an essence described by an attribute, then the author poses a division: does that attribute revert to the essence itself or to a meaning beyond the essence? The first has been refuted, so the second must be accepted, which is exactly the Ashʿarī/Māturīdī position that the attribute is additional to the essence. Thus, the indication of 'being knowing' is the same as the indication of having knowledge. The author then adds that deniers of accidents (like those who deny attributes) cannot even know an agent in the sensible world because they deny acts as accidents. Finally, he concludes that since he has already proven that the act indicates the agent's power and knowledge, it is unnecessary to discuss whether the indication is one or two.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalāla, indication)
عالم (ʿālim, knowing)
علم (ʿilm, knowledge)
ذات (dhāt, essence)
صفة (ṣifa, attribute)
شاهد (shāhid, witnessed world)
فعل محكم (fi'l muḥkam, well-ordered act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The act indicates the agent's being knowing separately from its indication of His knowledge, as if they are two different indications.
Correction: The act indicates both simultaneously because being knowing means having knowledge; they are the same indication.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the relationship between the act's indication of the agent being knowing and its indication of his knowledge?
A) They are two separate indications
B) The act indicates being knowing but not knowledge
C) They are the same indication, because being knowing means having knowledge
D) The act indicates neither

Key Takeaway
The act's indication of the agent's knowledge and knowing is one and the same, because knowing is nothing but having knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the created world, do you see it as a single unified act that reveals both God's knowledge and His being knowing as one, or do you sometimes separate His actions from His attributes? How can you train yourself to see His hand and His wisdom together in every event?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that the act only shows the agent is knowing without showing that He has knowledge. In your life, have you ever accepted God's actions while doubting His knowledge or wisdom in them? What specific situation challenges you to affirm that God's act flows from His perfect knowledge?
?
If knowing that God's act indicates both His knowledge and His being knowing is so central to true belief, what steps can you take today to let this truth transform how you respond to trials and blessings? Will you commit to seeing every act of God as a reminder that He is both all-knowing and the source of all knowledge?

The Mu'tazila Doubt About Eternal Attributes
Author introduces Mu'tazila objection about eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that multiple eternal attributes would imply multiple eternal entities, and why the author refers to a prior discussion among Māturīdi scholars.

Core Idea
Multiple eternal attributes do not necessarily mean multiple gods.

In this short passage, Imam al-Nasafī begins to refute a key doubt of the Mu'tazila: the claim that affirming eternal attributes for Allah would lead to affirming multiple eternal entities, which is impossible. He notes that this issue has already been discussed earlier in the book, and that there is disagreement among Māturīdi scholars on the precise answer. Although the passage is cut off, it sets the stage for the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author announces the refutation, quotes the Mu'tazila doubt, and then references a prior discussion.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila objection
Find the precise wording of the doubt: 'If Allah had attributes, they would be eternal entities, and the belief in eternal beings is impossible.' Understand why this is a serious challenge to the doctrine of eternal attributes.
The Mu'tazila equate 'eternal entity' with 'god', so multiple eternal entities would imply multiple gods.
3
Notice the author's reference
The author says the objection 'has already preceded in the course of our speech' and mentions 'the disagreement among our companions'. This indicates that the refutation builds on earlier discussions and that there is internal debate among Māturīdis.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important for Māturīdi theology to address this doubt? How does acknowledging internal disagreement strengthen the author's approach?
This doubt challenges the very concept of multiple divine attributes, so resolving it is crucial for defending orthodox theology.
