The first doubt he addresses is the classic objection: if God has multiple attributes, those attributes would be eternal entities (qadīmāt), and affirming multiple eternal beings is absurd. The author notes that this objection has already been discussed earlier in the book, and that there is disagreement among his own school (the Māturīdis) on how to answer it. The text cuts off before the full argument, but the orientation is clear: the author is starting the refutation by recalling a previous discussion.

The Mu'tazila saw multiple eternal attributes as problematic. In your own worship, do you find yourself focusing more on Allah's oneness in a way that downplays His attributes? How can you deepen both your tawhid and your appreciation for His specific attributes like mercy, power, and wisdom?

Questioning the Mu'tazila Distinction
Nasafi poses the initial question and dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial question posed by Nasafi to the Mu'tazila regarding the relationship between indicating the agent as knowing and indicating his knowledge.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila separate two indications that are actually one.

Nasafi begins his refutation by directly challenging the Mu'tazila claim that the act indicates the agent's being 'knowing' separately from indicating his knowledge. He asks them to clarify what 'being knowing' actually means: is it just the essence or something more? This sets up the argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the question
Read the Arabic passage where Nasafi asks the Mu'tazila why they separate the two indications. Notice the exact phrasing.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila claim that the act indicates the agent's attribute of being 'knowing' (ʿālim) independently of indicating his knowledge (ʿilm). This is the claim being questioned.
3
Analyze the question about 'being knowing'
Nasafi then asks: does 'being knowing' refer back to the essence alone or to a meaning beyond the essence? This dichotomy is the foundation of the argument.
Highlight the two options.
4
Reflect on the refutation of the first option
Nasafi quickly dismisses the first option (that it refers to the essence alone) as already invalidated. This sets the stage for the next lesson.
This is a key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لعامة المعتزلة : لم تقولون : إن دلالة الفعل على كون فاعله عالما غير الدلالة على ثبوت علمه ؟ وما معنى كونه عالما ؟ أراجع هو إلى الذات فحسب، أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن قلتم : إنه راجع إلى الذات فحسب فقد مر إبطاله. وإن قلتم : إنه راجع إلى ذات موصوف بصفة. قلنا: التقسيم قائم، فإنا نقول: يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى عين الذات أو على ذات موصوف بصفة وراء الذات؟ فإن قلتم : يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى عين الذات فقد مر إبطاله. وإن قلتم يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى معنى وراء الذات، فقد أقررتم بالحق. وتبين أن الدلالة على كونه عالما هي الدلالة على أن له علما، وما ذكر من الانفكاك ممنوع ، وما ذكر من الدليل باطل، فإنا لا نسلم أن نفاة الأعراض يعرفون في الشاهد فاعلا ، فإن المعرفة بالفاعل لن تتصور بدون المعرفة بالفعل، وهم لا يعرفون في الشاهد فعلا ليعرفوا به فاعلا وهذا لأن الفاعل في الشاهد لا يفعل الجسم إنما يفعل العرض، وهؤلاء ينكرون الاعراض فكيف يعرفون الفعل، وإذا لم يعرفوا الفعل لا يعرفوا الفاعل على أنا أقمنا الدلالة على أن الفعل المحكم يدل على قدرة الفاعل وعلمه، فبعد ذلك لا حاجة بنا إلى أن نبين أن الدلالة على العلم والعالم دلالة واحدة أو دلالتان. والله الموفق.
And it is said to the generality of the Mu'tazila: Why do you say that the indication of the act upon its agent being knowing is other than the indication upon the affirmation of His knowledge? And what is the meaning of His being knowing? Does it refer back to the essence alone, or to a meaning beyond the essence? If you say: It refers back to the essence alone, then its invalidation has already passed. And if you say: It refers back to an essence described by an attribute. We say: The division stands, for we say: Does it indicate an essence described by an attribute referable to the very essence, or an essence described by an attribute beyond the essence? If you say: It indicates an essence described by an attribute referable to the very essence, then its invalidation has already passed. And if you say: It indicates an essence described by an attribute referable to a meaning beyond the essence, then you have acknowledged the truth. And it is clear that the indication upon His being knowing is the indication upon His having knowledge. And what was mentioned of separation is denied, and what was mentioned of the proof is false. For we do not concede that the deniers of accidents (nufāt al-aʿrāḍ) know in the witnessed world (shāhid) an agent, for knowledge of the agent cannot be conceived without knowledge of the act. And they do not know in the witnessed world an act by which they might know an agent. This is because the agent in the witnessed world does not do the body; he only does the accident (ʿaraḍ). And these people deny accidents. So how can they know the act? And if they do not know the act, they do not know the agent. Moreover, we have established the proof that the well-made act indicates the power and knowledge of the agent. So after that, we have no need to show whether the indication upon knowledge and upon the knowing one is one indication or two. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that the act indicates the agent as being 'knowing' (ʿālim) separately from indicating that he has knowledge (ʿilm). Nasafi employs a division (taqsim) argument. First, he asks the Mu'tazila: what does it mean for the agent to be 'knowing'? Is it reducible to the essence alone, or is it an additional meaning (maʿnā) beyond the essence? If they say it is reducible to the essence, that has already been refuted. If they say it refers to an essence described by an attribute, then Nasafi poses a further division: does the act indicate an essence described by an attribute that is identical to the essence, or an essence described by an attribute that is beyond the essence? If the former, refuted; if the latter, they have admitted that the attribute is a real meaning beyond the essence, which entails that the act indicates that the agent has knowledge. Thus, the indication of being knowing is the same as the indication of having knowledge. Nasafi then addresses a counterargument: the Mu'tazila who deny accidents (aʿrāḍ) cannot even conceive of an agent in the witnessed world (shāhid) because they deny the acts (afʿāl) which are accidents. Therefore, their analogy from the witnessed world fails. Finally, Nasafi notes that since he has already established that a well-made act indicates the agent's power and knowledge, the question of whether the indication is one or two is moot.

Key Terms
دلالة
عالم
علم
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believe that 'being knowing' is a separate indication from having knowledge.
Correction: Nasafi argues that they cannot separate these because 'being knowing' ultimately means having knowledge.

Checkpoint
What two possibilities does Nasafi present for the meaning of 'being knowing'?
A) Essence alone or a meaning beyond essence
B) Knowledge alone or power alone
C) Eternal or temporal
D) Necessary or possible

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila must clarify what 'being knowing' means, and the only viable option is that it refers to a meaning beyond the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate Allah's knowledge, do you truly believe it is a real attribute distinct from His essence, or do you sometimes reduce it to a mere synonym for His being? How does that belief affect the awe you feel for Him? Examine your heart and repent if you have ever diminished His perfection.
?
The Mu'tazila separated what cannot be separated. In your own life, are there areas where your actions are detached from what you claim to know about Allah? Will you commit today to aligning your deeds with your knowledge, so that your life becomes a single consistent testimony?
?
Nasafi asks: 'What does it mean for Allah to be knowing?' Have you ever stopped to ponder the reality of His attributes beyond names? If not, what is holding you back from a deeper study of His names and attributes, and how can you start today?

The Division Argument (Taqsim)
Nasafi's division argument and its conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's division argument that forces the Mu'tazila to admit that the act indicates the agent's knowledge.

Core Idea
The division argument traps the Mu'tazila into admitting the attribute of knowledge.

Nasafi now lays out a precise division (taqsim). If the Mu'tazila say that 'being knowing' refers to an essence described by an attribute, he asks: does that attribute refer back to the essence itself or to something beyond? Whichever they choose leads to the same conclusion: the act indicates that the agent has knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division setup
Nasafi presents the second option from the previous lesson and then divides it further. Read the Arabic carefully.
2
Analyze the two branches
The first branch: the attribute is identical to the essence. This is dismissed. The second: the attribute is beyond the essence. This admits the truth.
3
See the conclusion
Once they admit the attribute is beyond the essence, they have admitted that the act indicates the agent's knowledge. Nasafi then denies any separation between the two indications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi forces a logical choice: either God's attribute of knowledge is identical to His essence (which leads to problems) or it is a real meaning beyond His essence. Do you live as though Allah's knowledge is a real, distinct attribute that sees every detail of your life, or do you act as if He doesn't fully know? What would change today if you truly believed that His knowledge is a separate, perfect reality?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to separate the indication of 'being knowing' from the indication of 'having knowledge'. In your own heart, have you separated the intellectual belief that Allah knows everything from the practical awareness that He sees your private moments? What specific habit or thought would you need to surrender to close that gap?
?
This argument shows that denying real attributes leads to contradiction. Are there areas of your life where you profess belief in Allah's attributes (like His knowledge or power) but functionally deny them by living as if He is not present or in control? What is one concrete step you can take this week to align your actions with the reality that Allah truly knows and sees you?

Critique of the Mu'tazila Denial of Accidents
Nasafi's critique of the Mu'tazila shahid analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the Mu'tazila who deny accidents cannot even know an agent in the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Deniers of accidents can have no knowledge of acts, thus no knowledge of agents.

Nasafi now takes a different approach. He argues that the Mu'tazila who deny the existence of accidents (aʿrāḍ) cannot consistently hold that they know an agent through acts in the witnessed world (shāhid), because acts are themselves accidents. Since they deny accidents, they cannot know any act, and thus cannot know any agent. This undermines their entire analogy from the created world.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Read the Arabic passage where Nasafi argues that deniers of accidents cannot know an agent.
2
Understand the logic
Knowledge of an agent requires knowledge of an act. But acts in the witnessed world are accidents. So if one denies accidents, one cannot know any act, and thus cannot know any agent.
3
Appreciate the final point
Nasafi then says he has already established that a well-made act indicates the agent's power and knowledge, so the question of whether the indication is one or two is moot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لعامة المعتزلة : لم تقولون : إن دلالة الفعل على كون فاعله عالما غير الدلالة على ثبوت علمه ؟ وما معنى كونه عالما ؟ أراجع هو إلى الذات فحسب، أم إلى معنى وراء الذات؟ فإن قلتم : إنه راجع إلى الذات فحسب فقد مر إبطاله. وإن قلتم : إنه راجع إلى ذات موصوف بصفة. قلنا: التقسيم قائم، فإنا نقول: يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى عين الذات أو على ذات موصوف بصفة وراء الذات؟ فإن قلتم : يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى عين الذات فقد مر إبطاله. وإن قلتم يدل على ذات موصوف بصفة راجعة إلى معنى وراء الذات، فقد أقررتم بالحق. وتبين أن الدلالة على كونه عالما هي الدلالة على أن له علما، وما ذكر من الانفكاك ممنوع ، وما ذكر من الدليل باطل، فإنا لا نسلم أن نفاة الأعراض يعرفون في الشاهد فاعلا ، فإن المعرفة بالفاعل لن تتصور بدون المعرفة بالفعل، وهم لا يعرفون في الشاهد فعلا ليعرفوا به فاعلا وهذا لأن الفاعل في الشاهد لا يفعل الجسم إنما يفعل العرض، وهؤلاء ينكرون الاعراض فكيف يعرفون الفعل، وإذا لم يعرفوا الفعل لا يعرفوا الفاعل على أنا أقمنا الدلالة على أن الفعل المحكم يدل على قدرة الفاعل وعلمه، فبعد ذلك لا حاجة بنا إلى أن نبين أن الدلالة على العلم والعالم دلالة واحدة أو دلالتان. والله الموفق.
And it is said to the generality of the Mu'tazila: Why do you say that the indication of the act upon its agent being knowing is other than the indication upon the affirmation of His knowledge? And what is the meaning of His being knowing? Does it refer back to the essence alone, or to a meaning beyond the essence? If you say: It refers back to the essence alone, then its invalidation has already passed. And if you say: It refers back to an essence described by an attribute. We say: The division stands, for we say: Does it indicate an essence described by an attribute referable to the very essence, or an essence described by an attribute beyond the essence? If you say: It indicates an essence described by an attribute referable to the very essence, then its invalidation has already passed. And if you say: It indicates an essence described by an attribute referable to a meaning beyond the essence, then you have acknowledged the truth. And it is clear that the indication upon His being knowing is the indication upon His having knowledge. And what was mentioned of separation is denied, and what was mentioned of the proof is false. For we do not concede that the deniers of accidents (nufāt al-aʿrāḍ) know in the witnessed world (shāhid) an agent, for knowledge of the agent cannot be conceived without knowledge of the act. And they do not know in the witnessed world an act by which they might know an agent. This is because the agent in the witnessed world does not do the body; he only does the accident (ʿaraḍ). And these people deny accidents. So how can they know the act? And if they do not know the act, they do not know the agent. Moreover, we have established the proof that the well-made act indicates the power and knowledge of the agent. So after that, we have no need to show whether the indication upon knowledge and upon the knowing one is one indication or two. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that the act indicates the agent as being 'knowing' (ʿālim) separately from indicating that he has knowledge (ʿilm). Nasafi employs a division (taqsim) argument. First, he asks the Mu'tazila: what does it mean for the agent to be 'knowing'? Is it reducible to the essence alone, or is it an additional meaning (maʿnā) beyond the essence? If they say it is reducible to the essence, that has already been refuted. If they say it refers to an essence described by an attribute, then Nasafi poses a further division: does the act indicate an essence described by an attribute that is identical to the essence, or an essence described by an attribute that is beyond the essence? If the former, refuted; if the latter, they have admitted that the attribute is a real meaning beyond the essence, which entails that the act indicates that the agent has knowledge. Thus, the indication of being knowing is the same as the indication of having knowledge. Nasafi then addresses a counterargument: the Mu'tazila who deny accidents (aʿrāḍ) cannot even conceive of an agent in the witnessed world (shāhid) because they deny the acts (afʿāl) which are accidents. Therefore, their analogy from the witnessed world fails. Finally, Nasafi notes that since he has already established that a well-made act indicates the agent's power and knowledge, the question of whether the indication is one or two is moot.

Misconception: That one can know an agent without knowing an act.
Correction: Nasafi states that knowledge of an agent cannot be conceived without knowledge of the act.

C) They deny the existence of agents
D) They deny the existence of the world

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila denial of accidents undermines their ability to ground any knowledge of agents in the witnessed world, making their argument self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, Nasafi exposes a fatal flaw in the Mu'tazila's reasoning. When you think about your own knowledge of Allah, do you rely on arguments that you have not fully scrutinized? Will you commit to examining each belief with the same rigor?
?
The Mu'tazila's denial of accidents led them to a self-defeating position. Are there any assumptions you hold about God that, if wrong, would collapse your entire understanding? What will you do to ensure your foundation is solid?
?
Nasafi's critique shows that a mistaken premise can invalidate an entire proof. Consider your daily acts of worship: are they built on a correct understanding of Allah's attributes, or are they tainted by imperfect analogies? How will you purify your intention and knowledge starting today?

Refuting the Objection of Multiple Eternals
Nasafi refutes the charge that attributes imply multiple eternals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection that divine attributes would become multiple eternals, and the correct meaning of 'qadim' when applied to attributes.

Core Idea
'Qadim' means prior in existence, not a self-subsistent god.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a key Mu'tazila objection: if God has attributes, those attributes would be eternal, and affirming multiple eternals is shirk. He shows that this objection misunderstands the term 'qadim'. By distinguishing two meanings within his own school and exposing the linguistic error of the Mu'tazila, he defends the orthodox view that attributes are eternal in the sense of having no beginning, not as independent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very structured argument here.
2
Identify the two responses from his school
The passage presents two positions among Nasafi's fellow scholars regarding applying 'qadim' to attributes. Find them and note how each avoids the objection.
One group avoids the term entirely; another uses it with a qualification.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila argument
What is the Mu'tazila claim that 'qadim' equals God? How does Nasafi refute it using linguistic evidence?
Nasafi cites Arabs calling a building 'qadim' meaning prior in existence.
4
Reflect on the correct formulation
Nasafi proposes that we say 'Allah is qadim with His attributes' and qualify each attribute as qadim in the sense of having no beginning.
This preserves tawhid while affirming eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نشتغل بحل شبهاتهم فنقول : قولهم لو كان الله صفات لكانت قديمات والقول بالقدماء محال قد سبق في خلال كلامنا الاختلاف بين أصحابنا في إطلاق لفظة القديم في الصفات فمن جعل القديم معنى وراء الذات لم يجوز إطلاق لفظة القديم على الصفات فاندفع عنه الإلزام - ومن قال منهم: إن القديم هو المتقدم في الوجود، أو الموجود الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده فإنه يقول : إن كل صفة قديمة غير أنه لا يجوز القول بالقدماء لئلا يسبق على وهم السامع أن كل قديم من القدماء قائم بذاته موصوف بصفات الألوهية بل نقول : ينبغي أن يقال الله تعالى قديم بصفاته، عند إطلاق لفظة القديم على كل صفة ينبغي أن يقيد، فيقال إن القديم القائم بالذات واحد وله صفات الكمال، وكل صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى وهي على معنى أن ليس لوجودها ابتداء، وأنها في الوجود متقدمة على المحدثات وأجمع، فلم قلتم : إن القول بقدماء على هذا الوجه محال وفيه وقع النزاع، وما يقوله الإسكافي والصالحي والجبائي من رؤساء القدرية أن القديم هو الله فالقول بالقدماء قول بالآلهة كلام في غاية الفساد فإن أحدا لا من أرباب اللغة ولا من أهل الكلام لم يقل إن القديم هو الله تعالى وسمعنا العرب تقول: هذا بناء قديم وشيخ قديم ويريدون به المتقدم في الوجود دون الوصف له بالألوهية.
Then we occupy ourselves with resolving their doubts and we say: Their statement, 'If God had attributes, they would be eternals (qadīmāt) and the affirmation of eternals (al-qudamāʾ) is impossible' — there has already preceded, in the course of our speech, the difference among our companions (aṣḥābinā) regarding the application of the term 'qadīm' to the attributes. For whoever makes 'qadīm' a meaning beyond the essence does not permit the application of the term 'qadīm' to the attributes, so the objection is repelled from him. And whoever among them says: 'al-qadīm is that which is prior in existence, or the existent that has no beginning to its existence' — then he says: Every attribute is qadīm, except that it is not permissible to say 'al-qudamāʾ' (the eternals) lest it occur to the mind of the hearer that every qadīm among the qudamāʾ is self-subsistent (qāʾim bi-dhātihī) described by the attributes of divinity. Rather, we say: It should be said, 'Allāh taʿālā is qadīm with His attributes.' When the term 'qadīm' is applied to each attribute, it should be qualified, so it is said: 'The qadīm that is self-subsistent is one, and He has attributes of perfection; and every attribute subsisting in the essence of Allāh taʿālā is on the meaning that its existence has no beginning, and that it is prior in existence to all originated things (al-muḥdathāt).' So why do you say that the affirmation of qudamāʾ in this manner is impossible? And therein lies the dispute. And what al-Iskāfī, al-Ṣāliḥī, and al-Jubbāʾī, leaders of the Qadariyya, say — that 'al-qadīm is Allāh, so affirming qudamāʾ is affirming gods' — is extremely corrupt speech. For no one, neither from the people of language nor from the people of kalām, has said that 'al-qadīm' is Allāh taʿālā exclusively. We have heard the Arabs say: 'This is a qadīm building' and 'a qadīm shaykh,' intending by it 'prior in existence,' without describing it with divinity.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila objection that if God has attributes, those attributes would be eternal (qadīma), leading to multiple eternals (qudamāʾ), which is impossible. Nasafi begins by noting that there is already a difference among his fellow scholars (aṣḥāb) regarding the application of the term 'qadīm' to the attributes. Some define 'qadīm' as a meaning beyond the essence, so they do not allow applying the term to attributes, thus the objection does not apply to them. Others define 'qadīm' as 'that which has no beginning to its existence.' For them, each attribute is qadīm in that sense, but they caution against using the plural 'qudamāʾ' because it might mislead the listener into thinking each eternal is a self-subsistent being with divine attributes. The correct expression is that Allāh is qadīm with His attributes, and each attribute is qadīm in the sense of having no beginning. Nasafi then attacks the Mu'tazila leaders who equate 'qadīm' with 'God', arguing that this is linguistically false because Arabs use 'qadīm' for buildings and old men, meaning only priority in existence, not divinity. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

Correction: Nasafi shows that 'qadīm' in Arabic simply means 'prior in existence' or 'without beginning', and is used for buildings and old men without any implication of divinity.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection fails because 'qadīm' does not mean 'god' but simply 'prior in existence' or 'without beginning', and attributes are qadīm in that sense without being independent beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What specific practice will you adopt to guard your heart against this misunderstanding?
?
The Mu'tazila built a whole objection on a linguistic error. Consider the words you use every day about your faith—are any of them clouded by assumptions or cultural baggage rather than precise theological meaning? Will you commit to studying the terms of tawḥīd with the depth that Nasafi shows here, so that your heart and tongue are aligned with the truth?
?
Nasafi uses the example of a 'qadīm building' to prove that the word does not imply divinity. How does this ordinary, concrete example help you see the importance of grounding theology in the Arabic language as understood by the Arabs? The Meaning of 'Qadim' in Language and Theology
Nasafi refutes the objection that applying 'qadim' to attributes implies multiple gods using linguistic and historical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'qadim' does not imply divinity and that attributing it to God's attributes does not lead to polytheism.

Core Idea
'Qadim' simply means 'old' or 'eternal', not 'god'.

The Mu'tazila argued that if we say God's attributes are qadim, we would have multiple eternal entities, implying multiple gods. Nasafi refutes this by clarifying the linguistic and theological meaning of 'qadim'. He shows that 'qadim' is a general term used for anything old or without beginning, and does not itself denote divinity. This lesson examines his linguistic evidence and the principle that the self-subsisting eternal is one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read Nasafi's initial refutation and his examples from Arabic usage and materialist belief. Note that he distinguishes between a self-subsisting eternal and attributes.
2
Identify the different meanings of 'qadim'
Understand that 'qadim' can refer to something prior in existence or without beginning, not necessarily divine. Notice the examples: building, sheikh, evil, and the materialists' view.
3
Reflect on the objection
Ask yourself: Why does the Mu'tazila claim that 'qadim' means God? How does Nasafi show this is false? This connects to the learning objective.

إطلاق لفظة القديم في الصفات فمن جعل القديم معنى وراء الذات لم يجوز إطلاق لفظة القديم على الصفات فاندفع عنه الإلزام - ومن قال منهم: إن القديم هو المتقدم في الوجود، أو الموجود الذي لا ابتداء لوجوده فإنه يقول: إن كل صفة قديمة غير أنه لا يجوز القول بالقدماء لئلا يسبق على وهم السامع أن كل قديم من القدماء قائم بذاته موصوف بصفات الألوهية بل نقول : ينبغي أن يقال الله تعالى قديم بصفاته، عند إطلاق لفظة القديم على كل صفة ينبغي أن يقيد، فيقال إن القديم القائم بالذات واحد وله صفات الكمال، وكل صفة قائمة بذات الله تعالى وهي على معنى أن ليس لوجودها ابتداء، وأنها في الوجود متقدمة على المحدثات قديمة أجمع، فلم قلتم : إن القول بقدماء على هذا الوجه محال وفيه وقع النزاع)، وما يقوله الإسكافي والصالحي والجبائي من رؤساء القدرية أن القديم هو الله فالقول بالقدماء قول بالآلهة كلام في غاية الفساد فإن أحدا لا من أرباب اللغة ولا من أهل الكلام لم يقل إن القديم هو الله تعالى وسمعنا العرب تقول: هذا بناء قديم وشيخ قديم ويريدون به المتقدم في الوجود دون الوصف له بالألوهية. ويقال في المثل السائر : الشر قديم، ولا يريدون أن الشر هو الله وأهل الدهر يعتقدون قدم كل جزء من أجزاء العالم، وما اعتقدوا ألوهيتها على أن هذا كلام يكفي منعه عن الاشتغال بإبطاله، فإنه يطالب بالدليل فيقال : لم قلت إن القديم هو الإله؟ والعقلاء بأسرهم على خلاف هذا على ما ذكرنا من إطلاق أهل اللغة لفظة القديم من غير إرادة الألوهية، واعتقاد أهل الدهر قدم العالم من غير اعتقاد الألوهية، ويسمون الأصنام آلهة وإن كانوا لا يعتقدون قدمها، فإنهم كانوا ينحتونها من الخشب والأحجار ويتخذونها من الصخر والنحاس، وهذه كلها من أجزاء العالم الذي اعتقدوا كونها مخلوقة لله تعالى على ما قال: ﴿ وَلَئِن سَأَلْتَهُم مَنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَّ اللهُ ﴾. وما يقولون : إن القدم من أخص أوصاف الباري، فلو كانت الصفة قديمة لوجدت المشاركة في أخص الأوصاف، فتكون الصفة مثلا الله تعالى، وإذا كانت مثلا له لكانا صفتين أو موصوفين، وكلا الأمرين باطل. هذا كلام صدر عن غاية الجهل، لأنهم بنوا هذا الكلام على مقدمة مجحودة، وبعد جريان المساهلة في تسليم المقدمة غفلوا عن شريطة المماثلة ومحلها، وبنوا علة المماثلة على أمر ممنوع، وأثبتوا حكم تلك العلة على أصل الخصوم دون أصلهم. فهذه أربع جهالات وقعوا فيها يستحيي عن الوقوع في واحدة منها الربض الخالي عن هذه الصناعة.
The application of the term 'qadim' (eternal/old) to the attributes. So whoever makes 'al-qadim' a meaning beyond the essence does not permit applying the term 'qadim' to the attributes, and the objection is repelled from him. And whoever among them says that 'al-qadim' is that which is prior in existence, or that which exists with no beginning to its existence, then he says: every attribute is qadim, but it is not permissible to speak of 'qudama'' (plural) lest it occur to the hearer's mind that every qadim among the qudama' is self-subsisting and described with attributes of divinity. Rather we say: it should be said that Allah is qadim with His attributes. When applying the term 'qadim' to every attribute, it should be qualified, and it is said that the qadim that is self-subsisting is one and has attributes of perfection, and every attribute subsists in Allah's essence, and it is in the sense that its existence has no beginning, and that it is in existence prior to originated things, all of them qadim. So why do you say that speaking of qudama' in this manner is impossible, and therein lies the dispute? And what al-Iskafi, al-Salihi, and al-Jubba'i, leaders of the Qadariyya, say—that 'al-qadim' is Allah, so speaking of qudama' is speaking of gods—is speech of extreme corruption. For no one among the people of language nor among the people of kalam has said that 'al-qadim' is Allah. We have heard the Arabs say: 'This is an old building' and 'an old sheikh,' and they intend by it priority in existence, not describing it with divinity. And it is said in the proverb: 'Evil is old,' and they do not mean that evil is Allah. And the materialists (ahl al-dahr) believe in the eternity of every part of the world, and they do not believe in their divinity. Moreover, this speech is sufficient to prevent preoccupation with refuting it, for it demands the proof: It is said: Why did you say that 'al-qadim' is God? All rational people are contrary to this, as we have mentioned of the people of language applying the term 'qadim' without intending divinity, and the materialists' belief in the eternity of the world without believing in divinity. And they call idols 'gods' even though they do not believe in their eternity, for they used to carve them from wood and stones and make them from rock and copper, and these are all parts of the world which they believed were created by Allah, according to what He said: {And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah}. And what they say: that qidam (eternity) is among the most specific attributes of the Creator, so if the attribute were qadim, there would be participation in the most specific attributes, so the attribute would be like Allah. And if it were like Him, they would be two attributes or two described entities, and both are false. This speech arises from extreme ignorance, because they built this speech on a rejected premise, and after leniency in conceding the premise, they neglected the condition of similarity (shart al-mumathala) and its place, and they based the cause of similarity on a forbidden matter, and they established the ruling of that cause according to the principle of the opponents, not according to their own principle. These are four ignorances they have fallen into; even a layperson (al-rabid) devoid of this craft would be ashamed to fall into one of them.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that God's attributes are eternal (qadim). The first objection is that if we say attributes are qadim, we would have multiple eternal entities (qudama'), implying multiple gods. The author responds by clarifying the meaning of 'qadim'. He notes that those who distinguish between essence and attributes do not apply the term 'qadim' to attributes, so the objection does not apply. For those who do apply it, they qualify it: the self-subsisting eternal is God alone, while attributes are eternal in the sense that they have no beginning and precede originated things. The objection that 'qadim' means God is baseless; linguistic usage shows 'qadim' is used for old things, not for gods. Examples: Arabs call a building qadim, and the proverb 'evil is qadim' does not deify evil. Materialists believe in the eternity of the world without considering it divine. Even idol worshippers call idols gods without believing them eternal. The second objection claims that qidam is a most specific attribute (akhass al-awsaf) of God, so sharing it makes attributes equal to God. The author refutes by pointing out four logical errors: (1) they build on a premise that is rejected (that qidam is exclusive to God); (2) after assuming the premise, they ignore the condition of similarity (shart al-mumathala), which requires that the shared attribute be considered in its full context; (3) they base the cause of similarity on a forbidden matter; (4) they apply the ruling to the opponents' principle rather than their own. The author concludes that these are four ignorances that even a mere novice would avoid.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim)
قدماء (qudama')
القدرية (al-Qadariyya)
الإسكافي (al-Iskafi)
الصالحي (al-Salihi)
الجبائي (al-Jubba'i)
الدهرية (al-dahriyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term 'qadim' necessarily means 'God' so calling attributes qadim implies multiple gods.
Correction: Nasafi shows that 'qadim' is a general term for 'old' or 'eternal' used for many things without implying divinity. Divinity requires self-subsistence and being the Creator, which is unique to Allah.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Nasafi give to show that 'qadim' does not mean 'God'?
A) He quotes the Quran saying Allah is qadim.
B) He cites Arab usage of 'qadim' for buildings and the proverb 'evil is old'.
C) He argues that attributes are not real.
D) He says only the Mu'tazila think 'qadim' means God.

Key Takeaway
The term 'qadim' does not denote divinity; it simply means 'eternal' or 'old', and applying it to attributes does not imply multiple gods.

The Logical Fallacy in the Most Specific Attribute Argument
Nasafi exposes four logical fallacies in the Mu'tazila argument that sharing qidam makes attributes equal to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical errors in the Mu'tazila argument that sharing the attribute of qidam makes God's attributes equal to God.

Core Idea
Sharing a most specific attribute does not imply equality; context and condition matter.

The Mu'tazila raised a second objection: since qidam is a most specific attribute of God, if attributes share it, they would be like God. Nasafi refutes this by exposing four logical errors in their reasoning. This lesson dissects those errors and shows how the argument fails on multiple levels.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the passage starting from 'وما يقولون : إن القدم من أخص الأوصاف...' and its translation. Understand the Mu'tazila's claim that sharing a most specific attribute implies likeness.
2
Identify the four ignorances
Nasafi lists four logical errors: (1) building on a rejected premise, (2) neglecting the condition of similarity, (3) basing the cause of similarity on a forbidden matter, (4) applying the ruling to the opponents' principle. Try to locate each in the passage.
3
Reflect on the condition of similarity
Consider why sharing one attribute does not make two things identical. For example, two things can be white without being the same. This is the 'shart al-mumathala'.

The application of the term 'qadim' (eternal/old) to the attributes. So whoever makes 'al-qadim' a meaning beyond the essence does not permit applying the term 'qadim' to the attributes, and the objection is repelled from him. And whoever among them says that 'al-qadim' is that which is prior in existence, or that which exists with no beginning to its existence, then he says: every attribute is qadim, but it is not permissible to speak of 'qudama'' (plural) lest it occur to the hearer's mind that every qadim among the qudama' is self-subsisting and described with attributes of divinity. Rather we say: it should be said that Allah is qadim with His attributes. When applying the term 'qadim' to every attribute, it should be qualified, and it is said that the qadim that is self-subsisting is one and has attributes of perfection, and every attribute subsists in Allah's essence, and it is in the sense that its existence has no beginning, and that it is in existence prior to originated things, all of them qadim. So why do you say that speaking of qudama' in this manner is impossible, and therein lies the dispute? And what al-Iskafi, al-Salihi, and al-Jubba'i, leaders of the Qadariyya, say—that 'al-qadim' is Allah, so speaking of qudama' is speaking of gods—is speech of extreme corruption. For no one among the people of language nor among the people of kalam has said that 'al-qadim' is Allah. We have heard the Arabs say: 'This is an old building' and 'an old sheikh,' and they intend by it priority in existence, not describing it with divinity. And it is said in the proverb: 'Evil is old,' and they do not mean that evil is Allah. And the materialists (ahl al-dahr) believe in the eternity of every part of the world, and they do not believe in their divinity. Moreover, this speech is sufficient to prevent preoccupation with refuting it, for it demands the proof: It is said: Why did you say that 'al-qadim' is God? All rational people are contrary to this, as we have mentioned of the people of language applying the term 'qadim' without intending divinity, and the materialists' belief in the eternity of the world without believing in divinity. And they call idols 'gods' even though they do not believe in their eternity, for they used to carve them from wood and stones and make them from rock and copper, and these are all parts of the world which they believed were created by Allah, according to what He said: {And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah}. And what they say: that qidam (eternity) is among the most specific attributes of the Creator, so if the attribute were qadim, there would be participation in the most specific attributes, so the attribute would be like Allah. And if it were like Him, they would be two attributes or two described entities, and both are false. This speech arises from extreme ignorance, because they built this speech on a rejected premise, and after leniency in conceding the premise, they neglected the condition of similarity (shart al-mumathala) and its place, and they based the cause of similarity on a forbidden matter, and they established the ruling of that cause according to the principle of the opponents, not according to their own principle. These are four ignorances they have fallen into; even a layperson (al-rabid) devoid of this craft would be ashamed to fall into one of them.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari/Maturidi position that God's attributes are eternal (qadim). The first objection is that if we say attributes are qadim, we would have multiple eternal entities (qudama'), implying multiple gods. The author responds by clarifying the meaning of 'qadim'. He notes that those who distinguish between essence and attributes do not apply the term 'qadim' to attributes, so the objection does not apply. For those who do apply it, they qualify it: the self-subsisting eternal is God alone, while attributes are eternal in the sense that they have no beginning and precede originated things. The objection that 'qadim' means God is baseless; linguistic usage shows 'qadim' is used for old things, not for gods. Examples: Arabs call a building qadim, and the proverb 'evil is qadim' does not deify evil. Materialists believe in the eternity of the world without considering it divine. Even idol worshippers call idols gods without believing them eternal. The second objection claims that qidam is a most specific attribute (akhass al-awsaf) of God, so sharing it makes attributes equal to God. The author refutes by pointing out four logical errors: (1) they build on a premise that is rejected (that qidam is exclusive to God); (2) after assuming the premise, they ignore the condition of similarity (shart al-mumathala), which requires that the shared attribute be considered in its full context; (3) they base the cause of similarity on a forbidden matter; (4) they apply the ruling to the opponents' principle rather than their own. The author concludes that these are four ignorances that even a mere novice would avoid.

شريطة المماثلة (shart al-mumathala)
جهالات (jihalat/ignorances)

Misconception: Sharing a most specific attribute makes two things identical such that the attribute would be a god.
Correction: Nasafi shows that sharing one attribute does not make two things identical because the condition of similarity requires full context, not just one property.

Refutation of the First Ignorance on Qidam's Exclusivity
Nasafi refutes the claim that qidam is the most specific attribute of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that qidam is the most specific attribute of God leads to circular reasoning, and how Nasafi refutes it using logical analysis and analogies.

Core Idea
A circular proof proves nothing; Nasafi exposes the flaw.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles the first of several ignorant arguments from opponents. They claim that qidam (eternity) is the most specific attribute of God, which would make attributes like knowledge and power not eternal. Nasafi shows that this premise is not self-evident but disputed, and that proving it leads to an impossible circularity. Let's see how he dismantles their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's initial claim
Read the first few lines. The opponents built their argument on the premise that qidam is among the most specific attributes of God. Note this claim.
This is the foundation of their argument.
2
See Nasafi's immediate rejection
Nasafi responds with 'This is denied' (ممنوع). He challenges them to justify why they say that.
He doesn't accept the premise as given.
3
Follow the opponents' justification
The opponents argue that 'existent' is general, so qidam is the most specific. Nasafi counters by pointing out that the dispute is about whether attributes are eternal themselves.
He reveals their premise is disputed (مقدمة مجحودة).
4
Understand the circular dependency
Nasafi shows that to prove their premise, they would need to prove that no eternal exists other than God's essence, which would already win the debate. Hence their argument is circular: proof depends on conclusion and vice versa.
This is the core logical flaw.
5
Reflect on the analogies
Nasafi gives two analogies: a man in a well who needs a rope to exit but cannot bring the rope without exiting, and a locked door with the key inside. These illustrate the impossibility of initiating the proof.
Analogies help grasp the circularity intuitively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان هذه الجهالات : أما الأولى منها فنقول : بنوا هذا الكلام على كون القدم من أخص أوصاف الباري جل وعلا وهذا ممنوع ، يقال لهم : لم قلتم ذلك؟ فإن :قالوا : قلنا ذلك لأن الموجود وصف عام يستحيل على القديم والمحدث، وإذا قيل: إنه تعالى قديم فهو وصف الموجود بصفة يختص بها عن غيره من الموجودات ثم ليس في القديم صفة أخص من القدم، وكان هو وصفا خاصا لا أخص منه. قيل: وهل وقع النزاع إلا في هذا؟ أليس أن خصومكم يقولون إن القديم إما أن يكون قائما بالذات وهو ذات الباري جل وعلا، وإما أن يكون غير قائم بالذات وهو صفاته تعالى فكان ،وصف، وعندكم لا أخص من القديم، فإذا هذه مقدمة مجحودة، لا يمكنهم إثبات هذه المقدمة إلا بإقامة الدليل على نفي قديم غير قائم بالذات، و اختصاص صفة القديم بالذات فحسب، ولو أمكنكم إثبات هذا لوقعت لكم الغنية عن التمسك بهذه المقدمة، فإذا هذا منكم تعلق بدليل لا يثبت صحته إلا بثبوت المدلول وكل دليل هذا سبيله فهو باطل، لأنه لا يمكن إثبات الملول إلا بثبوت الدليل، ولا يمكن إثبات الدليل إلا بثبوت المدلول فلا يتوصل إلى إثبات أحدهما البتة. كمن وقع في بئر ولا يمكنه الخروج إلا بإحضار الحبل، ولا يمكن إحضار الحبل إلا بخروجه فامتنع كل واحد منهما. أو كمن أقفل بابا وترك المفتاح في البيت، ولا يمكن فتح الباب إلا بإخراج المفتاح، ولا إخراج المفتاح إلا بفتح الباب، فامتنع الأمران جميعا فكذا هذا . والله الموفق.
And the explanation of these ignorances: As for the first of them, we say: They built this statement on the premise that qidam (eternity/preexistence) is among the most specific attributes of the Creator, Mighty and Majestic, and this is denied. It is said to them: Why did you say that? If they say: We said that because 'existent' is a general attribute that applies to both the eternal and the originated; and when it is said that He, exalted, is eternal, it is describing the existent with an attribute by which He is distinguished from other existents, and then there is no attribute in the eternal more specific than qidam, and it is a specific attribute with nothing more specific than it. It is said: And is the dispute not about this? Do your opponents not say that the eternal is either self-subsistent, which is the Essence of the Creator, or not self-subsistent, which are His attributes? And according to you there is nothing more specific than qidam. So this is a disputed premise. They cannot prove this premise except by adducing evidence for the negation of an eternal that is not self-subsistent and the restriction of the attribute of qidam to the Essence alone. And if you could prove this, you would have no need to cling to this premise. So this from you is clinging to a proof whose validity cannot be established except by establishing the conclusion, and every proof that is like this is invalid, because it is not possible to establish the conclusion except by establishing the proof, and it is not possible to establish the proof except by establishing the conclusion, so reaching the establishment of either is absolutely impossible. Like one who falls into a well and cannot get out except by bringing a rope, and cannot bring the rope except by getting out, so each is prevented. Or like one who locks a door and leaves the key inside the house, and cannot open the door except by taking out the key, and cannot take out the key except by opening the door, so both matters are prevented. So is this. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (رد) of the first ignorance. Nasafi begins by identifying the opponents' claim: they built their argument on the premise that qidam (eternity) is among the most specific attributes (أخص الأوصاف) of God. He immediately declares this premise as rejected (ممنوع). He then poses a challenge: 'Why do you say that?' The opponents respond that 'existent' is a general term applying to both eternal and originated, so when applied to God, qidam is the most specific distinguishing attribute. Nasafi counters by pointing out that the very dispute is about this: their opponents (presumably Ash'aris) hold that the eternal (قديم) includes both God's Essence (ذات) and His attributes (صفات)—the former self-subsistent, the latter not. For the current opponents, however, nothing is more specific than qidam. Thus their premise is disputed (مقدمة مجحودة). They cannot prove it without first proving that no eternal exists that is not self-subsistent (i.e., only God's Essence is eternal). But proving that would already win the argument, making the premise unnecessary. Hence their reasoning is circular: the proof's validity depends on the conclusion, and the conclusion depends on the proof, leading to an impossibility. Nasafi illustrates with two analogies: a person in a well needing a rope to exit but cannot bring the rope without exiting, and a locked door with the key inside—neither action can be initiated. Thus the opponents' argument is invalid. He ends with 'And God is the Giver of success.'

Key Terms
قدم
أخص الأوصاف
مقدمة مجحودة
دليل
مدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qidam is the most specific attribute of Allah, and this is an unassailable premise.
Correction: The premise is disputed; proving it leads to circular reasoning. The attributes themselves are also eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the main flaw Nasafi identifies in the opponents' first argument?
A) The premise that qidam is the most specific attribute is accepted by all.
B) The argument is circular because the proof depends on the conclusion and vice versa.
C) The opponents used an analogy that is incorrect.
D) The opponents denied the existence of God's attributes.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the first ignorance by exposing that its premise is disputed and leads to a circular argument, making it invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a belief you hold about God that you have never questioned, simply assuming it is obvious? What would it take for you to honestly examine whether that belief is actually disputed among scholars?
?
Nasafi uses the analogy of a man stuck in a well who cannot get the rope without getting out. In your spiritual life, are you relying on any circular reasoning—justifying a practice or belief with the very thing you are trying to prove? Identify one such loop and commit to breaking it by seeking a genuine foundation.
?
The opponents' mistake was to build their argument on an unproven premise they thought was self-evident. What 'self-evident' truth about your faith or life might actually be a disputed premise that you need to examine more carefully? Will you dare to test it, even if it is uncomfortable?

Divine Attributes

The Condition of Similarity: God and His Attributes
Nasafi explains why similarity between God and His attributes is impossible due to lack of dissimilarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim of similarity between God and His attributes is logically impossible, based on the requirement of dissimilarity between distinct entities.

Core Idea
Similarity requires two distinct things; God and His attributes are not distinct.

Nasafi continues his refutation of objections by addressing a second ignorance: the belief that the Creator could be similar to His attributes. He explains that similarity between any two things requires a condition of dissimilarity — they must be distinct. Since God’s Essence and attributes are not distinct in the way two separate entities are, the very condition for similarity is absent, making the claim impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very compact logical style.
2
Identify the condition and its locus
Find the condition (sharīṭa) of similarity and its locus (maḥall) as defined in the passage. Underline the key terms.
The condition is dissimilarity (mukhālafa) and the locus is two distinct things (mutaghāyirān).
3
Trace the argument's logical chain
Follow the reasoning: similarity requires condition → condition requires distinct entities → no distinctness between God and His attributes → therefore similarity is impossible.
This shows why the opponent's claim is rationally invalid.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the core point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجهالة الثانية وهي الجهل بشريطة المماثلة ومحلها. فهي أن المماثلة لن تثبت إلا بشريطة المغايرة بين المتماثلين، وكذا الموافقة والمخالفة هذه كلها أوصاف المتغايرين ومحلها المتغايران ولا مغايرة بين الذات والصفات على ما نبين بعد هذا ، فكانت شريطة المماثلة ومحلها معدومين، فكان القول بالمماثلة محالا.
As for the second ignorance, which is ignorance about the condition of similarity (mumāthala) and its locus. It is that similarity is not established except with the condition of dissimilarity (mukhālafa) between the similars. Likewise, agreement and opposition—all of these are attributes of distinct things and their locus is the two distinct things. And there is no dissimilarity between the Essence and the attributes, as we will explain after this. So the condition of similarity and its locus are nonexistent, so the statement of similarity is impossible.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the second ignorance. Nasafi identifies it as ignorance regarding the condition (sharīṭa) and locus (maḥall) of similarity (mumāthala). He explains that similarity between two things can only be established if there is a condition of dissimilarity (mukhālafa) between them—meaning the two must be distinct in some way. Similarly, agreement and opposition are attributes of things that are different from each other (mutaghāyirān). The locus of these relations is the two distinct entities. However, between God's Essence (dhāt) and His attributes (ṣifāt), there is no dissimilarity (no negation of common attribute? Actually 'lā mughāyara' means no difference/distinction) — as will be explained later. Since the condition (dissimilarity) and the locus (two distinct things) are both absent, the claim of similarity is impossible (muḥāl). Thus, it is invalid to argue that God and His attributes are similar in any respect.

Key Terms
مماثلة
شريطة
مغايرة
متغايران
ذات
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that similarity requires only common attributes without considering the distinction of subjects.
Correction: Nasafi emphasizes that similarity presupposes two distinct entities; without distinctness, the condition of similarity is absent.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi consider the claim of similarity between God and His attributes to be impossible?
A) Because God's attributes are not real.
B) Because similarity requires two distinct things, but God and His attributes are not distinct.
C) Because similarity requires equality in all respects.
D) Because the condition of similarity is unknown.

Key Takeaway
Similarity between God and His attributes is impossible because it requires two distinct things, and there is no distinction between God's essence and attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever thought of God's attributes as separate 'parts' of Him, like your own qualities are separate from your essence? How does the truth that there is no dissimilarity between His essence and attributes challenge that assumption?
?
If similarity between God and His attributes is impossible, what does that mean for how you understand the names of God you recite in du'a? Are you tempted to reduce them to mere labels?
?
When you say 'Allah is Knowing,' do you ever separate in your mind His knowledge from His very being? How can you train yourself to see His attributes as Him, not just things He has?

Responding to the Mu'tazila on the Subsistence of Attributes
Nasafi responds to the Mu'tazila objection on attribute subsistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection that the eternity of God's attributes leads to an impossible regress, and the position of the early proponents of attributes that God is eternal by His attributes without describing each attribute individually as eternal.

Core Idea
The early Salaf affirmed God's eternal attributes without calling each attribute 'eternal'.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi addresses a key Mu'tazila objection: if God has attributes, they must be eternal, but then they would require a subsistence that is impossible, or if they subsist by themselves, that violates Maturidi principles. Nasafi responds by citing the early scholars of the attributes school, who affirmed that God is eternal by His attributes but refrained from saying each attribute is individually eternal. This lesson unpacks that exchange and the subtle theological distinction it preserves.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Read the objection given in 'What they said...'. Understand their argument: if attributes are eternal, they need a subsistence that is impossible.
The objection is presented concisely; focus on the chain of reasoning.
2
Identify Nasafi's response
Nasafi responds by citing the early proponents of attributes (aṣḥāb al-ṣifāt). Note their specific phrasing.
This group includes the Kullābiyya and early Ash'aris.
3
Understand the key distinction
They say God is eternal by His attributes, but do not call each attribute eternal individually. Grasp why this nuance avoids the Mu'tazila objection.
It sidesteps the infinite regress problem.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this preserves the eternity of attributes without requiring an additional attribute of persistence.
This aligns with Maturidi principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن الله تعالى لو كانت له صفات لكانت باقيات، ويستحيل بقاؤها بالبقاء، وعندكم بقاؤها بذواتها غير جائز ولو جوزتم لهدمتم قاعدتكم. نجيب عن ذلك فنقول - والله الموفق إن قدماء أصحاب الصفات كانوا يقولون : إن الله تعالى باق بصفاته، وامتنعوا عن وصف كل صفة بالبقاء فلم يقولوا علمه باق، ولا حياته ولا قدرته، ويقولون : يثبت لنا بالدلائل أن الله تعالى صفات أزلية قائمة به ويثبت استحالة كون الباقي باقيا بلا بقاء بالدليل، ويثبت
And what they said: 'If God Most High had attributes, they would be eternal (bāqiyāt), and their subsistence/existing (baqā') by subsistence is impossible, and according to you their subsistence by their own essences is not permissible, and if you permitted it you would demolish your foundation.' We answer that by saying—and God is the grantor of success: The early proponents of attributes (aṣḥāb al-ṣifāt) used to say: God Most High is eternal (bāq) by His attributes, and they refrained from describing each attribute with subsistence (baqā'), so they did not say: His knowledge is permanent, nor His life, nor His power. And they say: It is established for us through proofs that God Most High has eternal attributes subsisting in Him, and it is established through proof the impossibility of an eternal thing being permanent without subsistence, and it is established...
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The Mu'tazila object: if God has attributes, those attributes must be eternal (bāqiyāt). But their being eternal/subsistent by subsistence (baqā') is impossible because subsistence is itself a meaning that cannot subsist in another meaning (a standard 'an-naqd'). And if the Maturidis claim attributes subsist by their own essences (i.e., without a separate attribute of persistence), that would undermine their own principle (that entitative attributes need a substrate). Imam Nasafi responds by citing the early followers of the attributes school (the Salaf and Kullābiyya). They say God is eternal by His attributes, but they refrain from describing each attribute individually as 'eternal' (i.e., they do not say 'His knowledge is eternal' as a separate description). Instead, they affirm that through proofs we know God has eternal attributes subsisting in Him, and also that it is impossible for an eternal thing to be permanent without subsistence. However, the text cuts off, so the complete resolution is not given here. The Maturidi position is that attributes are not qualified by persistence as separate entities; rather, they are eternal because God is eternal, and they subsist with Him without requiring a separate attribute of persistence.

Key Terms
صفات
بقاء
باق
أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think that if attributes are eternal, they must each be described as 'eternal' individually, leading to an infinite regress or impossibility.
Correction: The early scholars held that God is eternal by His attributes, but did not predicate eternity on each attribute separately, thus avoiding the regress.

Checkpoint
How did the early proponents of attributes (aṣḥāb al-ṣifāt) describe the eternity of God's attributes according to Nasafi?
A) They said each attribute is eternal by itself.
B) They said God is eternal by His attributes but did not call each attribute 'eternal' individually.
C) They denied the eternity of attributes entirely.
D) They claimed attributes are created.

Key Takeaway
Imam Nasafi defends the eternity of God's attributes by showing that the early Salaf affirmed that God is eternal by His attributes without predicating eternity on each attribute individually, thus avoiding the Mu'tazila's infinite regress objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The early Salaf refrained from saying 'His knowledge is eternal' even though they believed it. They chose theological caution over logical precision. Is there an area of your faith where you feel the urge to say more than what the revelation or the Salaf said? What would it cost you to adopt their restraint for the sake of preserving divine transcendence? Commit to identifying one such area and remain silent on it for a month.
?
The Mu'tazila's objection seemed logically airtight, yet the Salaf refused to follow the logic to its conclusion. Are you willing to accept theological boundaries even when your own reasoning pushes you to go further? When has your desire for intellectual consistency led you to overstep the limits of what the righteous predecessors said? Repent and resolve to submit to their guidance.

Mu'tazila Objection on Attributes and Early Reply
Mu'tazila object and author replies with early scholars' position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection regarding divine attributes and the reply based on the early scholars' approach.

Core Idea
Early scholars affirmed attributes without applying the term 'eternal' to each one.

In this passage, the author presents a classic Mu'tazila objection: if God has attributes, they must be eternal, but that leads to an impossibility regarding subsistence. He then replies by citing the early scholars' position that they affirmed the attributes but refrained from calling each attribute 'subsistent' (baqi). However, he notes that this reply is not unassailable, as opponents can still argue that perpetual attributes must be subsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Mu'tazila objection that if God has attributes, they must be eternal, but they cannot subsist by themselves or by an accident of subsistence.
Notice the logical trap: if attributes are eternal, they need some mode of subsistence.
2
Understand the logical problem
The Mu'tazila argue that the opponent's position is inconsistent: they cannot permit attributes to subsist by themselves (as that makes them independent) nor by subsistence (which would require an infinite regress).
3
Examine the early scholars' reply
The author replies that the early scholars affirmed God's attributes and their eternity, but they did not apply the term 'baqi' to each attribute individually. They refrained from saying 'God's knowledge is subsistent,' etc.
This is a terminological distinction: the attributes are eternal but not described as 'subsisting' separately.
4
Note the author's caveat
The author acknowledges that this reply is not conclusive, because opponents can argue that if attributes are perpetual (eternal past and future), they must be subsistent.
This shows the author's intellectual honesty about the difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثبات حكم العلة التي هي علة عندهم على الخصوص

اعتراض للمعتزلة: وما قالوا: إن الله تعالى لو كانت له صفات لكانت باقيات، ويستحيل بقاؤها بالبقاء، وعندكم بقاؤها بذواتها غير جائز ولو جوزتم لهدمتم قاعدتكم.

الرد عليه: نجيب عن ذلك فنقول - والله الموفق إن قدماء أصحاب الصفات كانوا يقولون : إن الله تعالى باق بصفاته، وامتنعوا عن وصف كل صفة بالبقاء فلم يقولوا علمه باق، ولا حياته ولا قدرته، ويقولون : يثبت لنا بالدلائل أن الله تعالى صفات أزلية قائمة به ويثبت استحالة كون الباقي باقيا بلا بقاء بالدليل، ويثبت أيضا استحالة قيام البقاء بالصفات بالدليل، وحصل من مجموع هذه الدلائل وجوب القول بالصفات، ووجوب الامتناع عن الوصف بكونها باقية إلا أن هذا الكلام يتعذر تمشيته، لأن الخصوم يقولون : إذ لم تكن الصفات دائمة فهي باقية وإن كانت دائمة بأن كانت موجودة في الأزل وهي للحال موجودة وتوجد أبدا لا إلى نهاية، فلن يتصور دائم لا يكون باقيًا.
Establishing the ruling of the cause which is a cause according to them in particular. Objection of the Mu'tazila: And they said: If God had attributes, they would be eternal (baqiyat), and it is impossible for them to subsist by subsistence (baqa'), and according to you their subsistence by themselves is not permissible, and if you permitted it you would demolish your foundation. The reply: We answer that by saying—and Allah grants success—that the early scholars of attributes used to say: God is eternal with His attributes, and they refrained from describing each attribute with subsistence, so they did not say His knowledge is subsistent, nor His life nor His power. And they say: It is established for us by proofs that God has eternal attributes subsisting in Him, and it is established that it is impossible for the subsistent to subsist without subsistence by proof, and it is also established that it is impossible for subsistence to inhere in the attributes by proof. And from the totality of these proofs there results the obligation of affirming the attributes, and the obligation of refraining from describing them as subsistent, except that this speech is difficult to carry through, because the opponents say: If the attributes are not perpetual, then they are subsistent, and if they are perpetual by being existent in pre-eternity and now existent and will exist forever without end, then one cannot conceive of a perpetual that is not subsistent.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents a classic Mu'tazila objection and the author's reply. The Mu'tazila argue that if God has attributes, they must be eternal (baqiya), but they cannot subsist by an accident of subsistence (as that would lead to infinite regress), nor can they subsist by themselves (as that would make them independent of God). They claim the opponent's position is inconsistent. The reply cites the early scholars (the Salaf) who affirmed that God is eternal with His attributes but did not apply the term 'subsistent' (baqi) to each attribute individually. They argued that proofs establish that attributes are eternal and subsisting in God, but also that it is impossible for subsistence to inhere in the attributes. The author then notes that this reply is not unassailable, because the opponents can still argue that if attributes are perpetual, they must be subsistent.

Correction: The author is not denying the eternity of attributes; rather, he is refusing to apply the term 'baqi' to each attribute individually for specific terminological and rational reasons.

Al-Ash'ari on Attribute Subsistence and Its Refutation
Al-Ash'ari's claim and its refutation through analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's argument that attributes subsist by the subsistence of the essence and why the author refutes it using the analogy of substance and accident.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's view on attribute subsistence leads to contradictions when applied to the created world.

This passage presents a critical exchange: al-Ash'ari attempts to preserve the subsistence of attributes by linking it to the subsistence of the essence. The author then challenges this by extending the same logic to the physical world, revealing inconsistencies. The debate hinges on the relationship between substance and accident.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ash'ari's position
Read the first part where al-Ash'ari states his view that attributes are subsistent by the subsistence of God's essence. Notice how he avoids saying they subsist without a subsistence.
Al-Ash'ari is responding to a previous objection.
2
Understand the reasoning
Al-Ash'ari argues that the subsistence of the essence covers both the essence and the attributes. Grasp the idea that the subsistence is not separate from the essence.
3
Identify the refutation's analogy
The author presses the argument by applying the same logic to the physical world: if the subsistence of a substance were subsistence for its accidents, then accidents would become other than the substance, leading to contradictions.
The analogy is key to the refutation.
4
Analyze the conclusion
The author concludes that if the substance's subsistence is impossible, then neither the non-existence nor existence of accidents alongside it is possible. Understand the force of this reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام للأشعري في الصفات والرد عليه

فاشتغل الأشعري بجواب هذا وقال : أنا أسلم أن علم الله تعالى باق وكذا سائر صفاته ولا أقول إنها باقية بلا بقاء ليلزمني هدم أصلي بل أقول: إنها باقية ببقاء، فإن بقاء ذات الله تعالى بقاء للذات وبقاء للصفات أيضا، وبقاء الذات بقاء لنفسه أيضا لأنه ليس غير الذات. فإذا ألزم عليه القول ببقاء الأعراض بأن يقال إن في الشاهد البقاء القائم بالذات ينبغي أن يصير بقاء لجميع صفات ذلك الذات فتصير الأعراض القائمة بالجواهر غير الجواهر، والبقاء القائم بشيء لا يكون بقاء لما تصورت المغايرة بين الجوهر وبين الصفات ولا عدمها للأعراض القائمة به لما تصورت المغايرة بين الجوهر مع بقاء الجوهر، لما أن بقاء الجوهر بقاء لهما، فما دام الجوهر باقيا كان بقاؤه موجودا، وكان بقاؤه بقاء لأعراضه والقول بعدم ما وجد بقاؤه محال، وإذا استحال بقائه استحال عدمها مع وجوده، ويستحيل أيضا وجودها مع عدمه إذ لا مع عدمها
Speech of al-Ash'ari on the attributes and the refutation of it. So al-Ash'ari occupied himself with answering this and said: I concede that the knowledge of God is subsistent, and likewise His other attributes, and I do not say that they are subsistent without subsistence lest my foundation be demolished, but I say they are subsistent by a subsistence. Indeed, the subsistence of the essence of God is a subsistence for the essence and also a subsistence for the attributes, and the subsistence of the essence is a subsistence for itself because it is not other than the essence. So if he is pressed by the issue of the subsistence of accidents by it being said that in the witnessed world, the subsistence inhering in the essence should become subsistence for all the attributes of that essence, so that the accidents subsisting in substances become other than the substances, and the subsistence inhering in a thing is not a subsistence for [something else] because distinction is perceived between the substance and the attributes, and not their non-existence for the accidents subsisting in it because distinction is perceived between the substance with the subsistence of the substance, because the subsistence of the substance is subsistence for both. So as long as the substance is subsistent, its subsistence is existent, and its subsistence is subsistence for its accidents, and saying that what has been found to subsist does not exist is impossible. And if its subsistence is impossible, then their non-existence along with its existence is impossible, and also their existence along with its non-existence is impossible, since there is no meaning with their non-existence.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ash'ari's position on the subsistence of divine attributes and the author's refutation. Al-Ash'ari asserts that the attributes are subsistent by a subsistence (baqa'), arguing that the subsistence of God's essence is also subsistence for the attributes, and that the subsistence of the essence is not other than the essence. The author then refutes this by drawing an analogy with the physical world: if we apply this reasoning, the subsistence inhering in a substance would have to be subsistence for all its accidents, making the accidents other than the substance. Moreover, the subsistence of a substance cannot be subsistence for something else due to the distinction between substance and attributes. This leads to contradictions: if the subsistence of the substance is impossible, then the non-existence of the accidents along with the existence of the substance is also impossible, and vice versa. The argument is dense and relies on metaphysical principles of substance and accident.

Key Terms
بقاء
ذات
أعراض
جوهر
مغايرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ash'ari's view on attribute subsistence is standard Sunni theology without issues.
Correction: The author (Maturidi) shows that al-Ash'ari's position leads to logical absurdities when applied to the created world, indicating it is not a sound theological stance.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari in this passage, what is the subsistence of the attributes?
A) They are subsistent without subsistence.
B) They are subsistent by the subsistence of God's essence.
C) They are not subsistent at all.
D) They are subsistent by a subsistence distinct from the essence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's attempt to ground attribute subsistence in essence subsistence is refuted by showing that the same logic in the physical world leads to impossible consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a theological argument without checking whether it leads to contradictions in other areas of belief? Consider a time you held a view about God's attributes that you later found to be inconsistent. What did you do with that discomfort? Are you willing to let go of a cherished teaching if logic shows it leads to absurdity?
?
This lesson shows that even al-Ash'ari, a great scholar, made a logical error. Does that shake your confidence in any scholar you follow? Ask yourself: is your faith built on following a person or on the truth itself? If the truth required you to disagree with a revered teacher, would you have the courage to do so?
?
The refutation uses an analogy from the physical world to critique a theological claim. How does the relationship between the created world and divine truths affect your understanding of God? Are you comfortable using physical analogies to discuss God? Or do you think such comparisons are inappropriate? Examine your own assumptions: are they based on careful reasoning or on mere acceptance?
