Al-Ash'ari's Claim and the Substance-Accident Analogy
Al-Nasafi presents al-Ash'ari's argument and the initial objection from the opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ash'ari's argument that divine attributes are eternal by God's own eternity and the initial objection based on the substance-accident analogy.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari's solution risks making accidents eternal like substances.

Al-Ash'ari attempted to maintain that God's attributes are eternal without making them independent beings. He claimed they are eternal by God's own eternity. But this leads to a problem: in the created world, the eternity of a substance makes its accidents eternal as long as it exists. This raises questions about the distinction between essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the background
The passage begins by stating that proofs require affirming attributes but forbid calling them 'eternal' (bāqiya). The opponents then raise a dilemma: if attributes are perpetual (dā'ima), they must be eternal.
2
Examine al-Ash'ari's response
Al-Ash'ari grants that attributes are eternal, but not by themselves—they are eternal by God's own eternity (baqā'). He insists that God's eternity covers both essence and attributes.
3
Follow the objection
The objection uses the analogy of substance and accident in the visible world: the eternity of a substance should make its accidents eternal. If so, accidents become 'other' than substances. If not, the analogy breaks.
Notice the logical move: al-Ash'ari is pressed on whether his view implies accidents are eternal by essence or by another's eternity.
4
Grasp the conclusion of this section
The passage concludes that if the substance's eternity does not make accidents eternal, then the definition of 'mutual otherness' (ghayriyya) becomes invalid. This sets up the deeper critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضا استحالة قيام البقاء بالصفات بالدليل، وحصل من مجموع هذه الدلائل وجوب القول بالصفات، ووجوب الامتناع عن الوصف بكونها باقية إلا أن هذا الكلام يتعذر تمشيته، لأن الخصوم يقولون : إذ لم تكن الصفات دائمة فهي باقية وإن كانت دائمة بأن كانت موجودة في الأزل وهي للحال موجودة وتوجد أبدا لا إلى نهاية، فلن يتصور دائم لا يكون باقيًا. كلام للأشعري في الصفات والرد عليه فاشتغل الأشعري بجواب هذا وقال : أنا أسلم أن علم الله تعالى باق وكذا سائر صفاته ولا أقول إنها باقية بلا بقاء ليلزمني هدم أصلي بل أقول: إنها باقية ببقاء، فإن بقاء ذات الله تعالى بقاء للذات وبقاء للصفات أيضا، وبقاء الذات بقاء لنفسه أيضا لأنه ليس غير الذات. فإذا ألزم عليه القول ببقاء الأعراض بأن يقال إن في الشاهد البقاء القائم بالذات ينبغي أن يصير بقاء لجميع صفات ذلك الذات فتصير الأعراض القائمة بالجواهر غير الجواهر، والبقاء القائم بشيء لا يكون بقاء لما تصورت المغايرة بين الجوهر وبين الصفات ولا عدمها للأعراض القائمة به لما تصورت المغايرة بين الجوهر مع بقاء الجوهر، لما أن بقاء الجوهر بقاء لهما، فما دام الجوهر باقيا كان بقاؤه موجودا، وكان بقاؤه بقاء لأعراضه والقول بعدم ما وجد بقاؤه محال، وإذا استحال بقائه استحال عدمها مع وجوده، ويستحيل أيضا وجودها مع عدمه إذ لا مع عدمها قيام للأعراض بدون الجوهر ، فصار كل جوهر مع ما له من الأعراض مما يستحيل وجود أحدهما مع انعدام صاحبه فبطل حد الغيرين وحيث رأينا أن الأعراض تغاير الجواهر التي تقوم بها، وأنها تنعدم مع بقاء الذات، علم أن بقاء الجوهر لم يكن بقاء لما قام به من الأعراض وفي حق الغائب مثل هذا الدليل منعدم إلا أنا نقول : إن ما ذكره الأشعري فاسد، لأن الخصوم يقولون : إن الصفة عندكم إن لم تكن عين الذات فلم جعلتم البقاء القائم بالذات بقاء لما ليس بالذات ولما لم يقم به البقاء؟ ألا ترى أن بقاء الجوهر يُحصل بقاء لأعراضه، وإنما لم يكن كذلك لأن العرض ليس هو الجوهر لا لما أنه غيره بل وصف المغايرة فصل في الباب، لأنه لا يمكن أن يقال: إن بقاء الجوهر لم يصر بقاء لما قام به من العرض لأنه غيره لأنه إنما كان غيرا له، لأن بقاء الجوهر لم يجعل بقاء له فانعدم مع وجود الجوهر وكان غيره، ولو جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء لأعراضه لما صات أغيارا له لأنها حينئذ كانت لا تنعدم مع بقاء الجوهر ، فإذا كان السبب لكونها أغيارا للجوهر امتناع جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء لها، فمن جعل كونها أغيارا له علة لامتناع جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء للأعراض فقد قلب القصة. والذي يحقق فساد ما ذهب إليه الأشعري أن بقاء الله تعالى لو كان بقاء لأنها ليست بأغيار له - للزم أن تكون حياته حياة لذاته ولجميع صفاته، لأنها ليست بأغيار له، وكذا قدرته وعلمه وسمعه وبصره فتصير كل صفة حية قادرة عالمة سميعة بصيرة مريدة، وهذا هو وصف الربوبية فتصير كل صفة موصوفة بالألوهية والربوبية فتؤدي إلى القول بالآلهة وهذا كفر وإبطال للتوحيد.
Also the impossibility of eternity (al-baqā') subsisting in the attributes by proof, and from the totality of these proofs follows the necessity of affirming the attributes, and the necessity of refraining from describing them as eternal (bāqiya), except that this speech is difficult to carry through, because the opponents say: If the attributes are not perpetual (dā'ima) then they are eternal, and if they are perpetual in that they exist in pre-eternity and are now existing and will exist forever without end, then it is inconceivable that something perpetual should not be eternal. Statement of al-Ash'arī on the attributes and the refutation of it. So al-Ash'arī occupied himself with answering this and said: I grant that God's knowledge is eternal, and likewise the rest of His attributes, and I do not say that they are eternal without an eternity (bi-lā baqā') lest it demolish my principle, but rather I say: they are eternal by an eternity (bi-baqā'), for the eternity of God's essence (dhāt) is an eternity for the essence and an eternity for the attributes as well, and the eternity of the essence is an eternity for itself also because it is not other than the essence. So if he is pressed with the affirmation of the eternity of accidents by saying that in the witnessed world the eternity subsisting in a substance should become an eternity for all the attributes of that substance, then the accidents subsisting in substances become other than the substances, and the eternity subsisting in a thing is not an eternity for what the distinction (al-mughāyara) between the substance and the attributes is conceived, nor is their non-existence for the accidents subsisting in it because the distinction between the substance and the non-existence of the accidents is conceived along with the eternity of the substance, because the eternity of the substance is an eternity for both, so as long as the substance remains eternal, its eternity exists, and its eternity is an eternity for its accidents, and the statement that what has its eternity existent is non-existent is absurd, and if its non-existence is impossible, then their non-existence together with its existence is impossible, and also their existence together with its non-existence is impossible, since there is no subsistence of accidents without the substance. So every substance together with its accidents is such that the existence of one of the two with the non-existence of the other is impossible, so the definition of mutual otherness (al-ghayriyya) becomes invalid. And where we see that accidents are other than the substances in which they subsist, and that they become non-existent while the substance remains, we know that the eternity of the substance was not an eternity for what subsists in it of accidents, and concerning the unseen such a proof is absent. However we say: What al-Ash'arī mentioned is corrupt, because the opponents say: The attribute according to you, if it is not the essence itself, then why did you make the eternity subsisting in the essence an eternity for what is not the essence and for what the eternity does not subsist in? Do you not see that the eternity of a substance produces eternity for its accidents, and it was not so only because the accident is not the substance, not because it is other than it, but rather the description of otherness is a separate chapter in the discussion, because it cannot be said that the eternity of the substance did not become an eternity for what subsists in it of the accident because it is other than it, since it only became other than it because the eternity of the substance did not make it eternal, so it became non-existent while the substance existed and was other than it. And if the eternity of the substance had made its accidents eternal, they would not have become other than it because then they would not become non-existent with the eternity of the substance. So if the reason for their being other than the substance is the prevention of making the eternity of the substance an eternity for them, then whoever makes their being other than it a cause for the prevention of making the eternity of the substance an eternity for the accidents has reversed the story. And what confirms the corruption of what al-Ash'arī held is that if the eternity of God were an eternity because the attributes are not other than Him, then it would necessitate that His life be a life for His essence and for all His attributes, because they are not other than Him, and likewise His power, knowledge, hearing, sight, will, so that every attribute would become living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing, willing, and this is the description of lordship (al-rubūbiyya), so that every attribute would be described with divinity (al-ulūhiyya) and lordship, leading to the affirmation of gods, and this is disbelief and nullification of tawḥīd.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ash'ari's position on whether the divine attributes are eternal by virtue of inheriting God's own eternity (baqā'). The passage proceeds as follows: First, it states the background: proofs necessitate affirming attributes for God, but also forbidding describing them as 'eternal' (bāqiya) in a way that might imply they are eternal by themselves. However, opponents argue that if attributes are perpetual (dā'ima) — existing in pre-eternity and forever — then they must be eternal (bāqiya), as a perpetual thing cannot be non-eternal. Then al-Ash'ari's answer is presented: He accepts that God's attributes are eternal, but not without an eternity (i.e., not self-subsisting), rather they are eternal by an eternity that is God's own eternity. He claims that God's eternity is an eternity for His essence and for His attributes, and for His essence alone since it is not other than the essence. Then al-Nasafi raises an objection: if we say that in the witnessed world the eternity subsisting in a substance becomes eternity for all its attributes (accidents), then accidents become other than substances; but if the eternity of the substance does not make the accidents eternal, then they are other. However, al-Ash'ari's argument is accused of being 'corrupt' (fāsid) because the opponents can ask: since the attribute, according to you, is not identical to the essence, why do you make the eternity of the essence apply to what is not the essence? The example of substance and accident is analyzed: the eternity of a substance does make its accidents eternal in the sense that they coexist as long as the substance exists, but they are not eternal in themselves; they cease when the substance ceases. The fact that accidents are 'other' (ghayr) than the substance is not a reason for the eternity not to apply; rather, it is because the substance's eternity does not preserve them that they become other. So al-Ash'ari has reversed the causal relationship. Finally, the strongest refutation: if God's eternity were applied to attributes simply because they are not other than Him, then every attribute would share all divine attributes (life, power, etc.) and become a god — this leads to polytheism.

Key Terms
بقاء
صفات
جوهر
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari's view is self-consistent and avoids problems with the substance-accident analogy.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Ash'ari's view leads to a paradox: if God's eternity makes attributes eternal, then in the created world a substance's eternity should make its accidents eternal, which contradicts the observed fact that accidents perish while substances remain.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what problem does the substance-accident analogy create for al-Ash'ari's view?
A) It shows that accidents are eternal like substances.
B) It forces the conclusion that accidents are not really other than substances, invalidating the definition of mutual otherness.
C) It proves that God's essence and attributes are identical.
D) It demonstrates that attributes are created.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's attempt to ground attribute eternity in God's essence fails because the substance-accident analogy shows it would erase the distinction between essence and attributes in the created world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defended a belief about Allah using reasoning that later felt forced or contradictory? What would it take for you to abandon that reasoning and submit to the truth, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
Al-Ash'ari's intention was to protect the eternity of Allah's attributes, but his analogy led to logical trouble. Reflect on an area of your own life where a well-intentioned practice or belief might actually be undermining your tawhid. Will you examine it critically today?
?
The passage shows that even subtle errors in reasoning about Allah can erase the distinction between Creator and creation. When was the last time you confused something about yourself with something about Allah? How can this lesson guard your heart against that confusion?

Divine Attributes
advanced
12 min
The Corruption of Al-Ash'ari's Reasoning and the Polytheism Consequence
Al-Nasafi provides the decisive refutation of al-Ash'ari's argument and shows it leads to polytheism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's deeper critique that al-Ash'ari's reasoning is corrupt and leads to the conclusion that every attribute would be a god, thus nullifying tawḥīd.

Core Idea
If God's eternity makes attributes eternal, each attribute becomes a god.

Al-Nasafi continues his refutation by showing that al-Ash'ari's argument is fundamentally flawed. He argues that the opponents can reverse al-Ash'ari's logic: the reason accidents are 'other' than substances is precisely because the substance's eternity does not make them eternal. Moreover, al-Nasafi delivers a devastating blow: if God's eternity applies to attributes simply because they are not other than Him, then every attribute would inherit all divine attributes, leading to polytheism.

Guided Path
1
Examine the refutation's structure
Al-Nasafi first declares that what al-Ash'ari mentioned is 'corrupt' (fāsid) and then gives two arguments: a logical reversal and a theological consequence.
2
Understand the logical reversal
The opponents argue that al-Ash'ari has reversed the causal relationship: it is not because accidents are other that the substance's eternity doesn't apply; rather, because the eternity doesn't apply, they become other.
This is a key dialectical move: the attribute's otherness is a result, not a cause.
3
Grasp the polytheism consequence
The final and strongest refutation: if God's eternity covers all attributes because they are not other, then each attribute would be described with all divine attributes (life, power, knowledge, etc.), making each attribute a god.
This is the climax: al-Ash'ari's view, if followed consistently, leads to multiple gods and nullifies tawḥīd.
4
Reflect on the severity
Note that al-Nasafi calls this 'kufr' and 'ibṭāl al-tawḥīd' — disbelief and nullification of monotheism. This shows the high stakes of the debate.

أيضا استحالة قيام البقاء بالصفات بالدليل، وحصل من مجموع هذه الدلائل وجوب القول بالصفات، ووجوب الامتناع عن الوصف بكونها باقية إلا أن هذا الكلام يتعذر تمشيته، لأن الخصوم يقولون : إذ لم تكن الصفات دائمة فهي باقية وإن كانت دائمة بأن كانت موجودة في الأزل وهي للحال موجودة وتوجد أبدا لا إلى نهاية، فلن يتصور دائم لا يكون باقيًا. كلام للأشعري في الصفات والرد عليه فاشتغل الأشعري بجواب هذا وقال : أنا أسلم أن So every substance together with its accidents is such that the existence of one of the two with the non-existence of the other is impossible, so the definition of mutual otherness (al-ghayriyya) becomes invalid. Do you not see that the eternity of a substance produces eternity for its accidents, and it was not so only because the accident is not the substance, not because it is other than it, but rather the description of otherness is a separate chapter in the discussion, because it cannot be said that the eternity of the substance did not become an eternity for what subsists in it of the accident because it is other than it, since it only became other than it because the eternity of the substance did not make it eternal, so it became non-existent while the substance existed and was other than it. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ash'ari's position on whether the divine attributes are eternal by virtue of inheriting God's own eternity (baqā'). The passage proceeds as follows: First, it states the background: proofs necessitate affirming attributes for God, but also forbidding describing them as 'eternal' (bāqiya) in a way that might imply they are eternal by themselves. However, opponents argue that if attributes are perpetual (dā'ima) — existing in pre-eternity and forever — then they must be eternal (bāqiya), as a perpetual thing cannot be non-eternal. Then al-Ash'ari's answer is presented: He accepts that God's attributes are eternal, but not without an eternity (i.e., not self-subsisting), rather they are eternal by an eternity that is God's own eternity. He claims that God's eternity is an eternity for His essence and for His attributes, and for His essence alone since it is not other than the essence. Then al-Nasafi raises an objection: if we say that in the witnessed world the eternity subsisting in a substance becomes eternity for all its attributes (accidents), then accidents become other than substances; but if the eternity of the substance does not make the accidents eternal, then they are other. However, al-Ash'ari's argument is accused of being 'corrupt' (fāsid) because the opponents can ask: since the attribute, according to you, is not identical to the essence, why do you make the eternity of the essence apply to what is not the essence? The example of substance and accident is analyzed: the eternity of a substance does make its accidents eternal in the sense that they coexist as long as the substance exists, but they are not eternal in themselves; they cease when the substance ceases. The fact that accidents are 'other' (ghayr) than the substance is not a reason for the eternity not to apply; rather, it is because the substance's eternity does not preserve them that they become other. So al-Ash'ari has reversed the causal relationship. Finally, the strongest refutation: if God's eternity were applied to attributes simply because they are not other than Him, then every attribute would share all divine attributes (life, power, etc.) and become a god — this leads to polytheism.

Divine Attributes

Refuting al-Ash'ari on the Permanence of Divine Attributes
Refutation of al-Ash'ari's argument that God's permanence applies to His attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Ash'ari's argument that God's permanence applies to His attributes is refuted and how it leads to polytheism.

Core Idea
If God's permanence extended to His attributes, each attribute would become a separate god.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes al-Ash'ari's claim that God's attribute of permanence (baqa') extends to all divine attributes. He first critiques the conceptual basis of 'otherness' between substances and accidents, then applies a similar reasoning to God's attributes, showing that if God's permanence were to include His attributes, each attribute would become a divine entity, leading to polytheism. This lesson walks through his incisive argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the text's own reasoning come through.
Notice the dense logical style typical of kalam texts.
2
Understand the substance-accident analogy
The author uses the observable relationship between substances and accidents to illustrate how permanence does not transfer.
Accidents cease while substance remains, so the substance's permanence is not for its accidents.
3
Identify the critique of al-Ash'ari's reversal
Al-Ash'ari reversed causality by making 'otherness' the reason for not applying permanence, whereas it is the result of the lack of permanence.
4
Grasp the polytheism consequence
If God's permanence applied to attributes, each attribute would possess all divine attributes, becoming a separate god.
This nullifies tawhid.

Source Text

فبطل حد الغيرين وحيث رأينا أن الأعراض تغاير الجواهر التي تقوم بها، وأنها تنعدم مع بقاء الذات، علم أن بقاء الجوهر لم يكن بقاء لما قام به من الأعراض وفي حق الغائب مثل هذا الدليل منعدم إلا أنا نقول : إن ما ذكره الأشعري فاسد، لأن الخصوم يقولون : إن الصفة عندكم إن لم تكن عين الذات فلم جعلتم البقاء القائم بالذات بقاء لما ليس بالذات ولما لم يقم به البقاء؟ ألا ترى أن بقاء الجوهر يحصل بقاء لأعراضه، وإنما لم يكن كذلك لأن العرض ليس هو الجوهر لا لما أنه غيره بل وصف المغايرة فصل في الباب، لأنه لا يمكن أن يقال: إن بقاء الجوهر لم يصر بقاء لما قام به من العرض لأنه غيره لأنه إنما كان غيرا له، لأن بقاء الجوهر لم يجعل بقاء له فانعدم مع وجود الجوهر وكان غيره، ولو جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء لأعراضه لما صات أغيارا له لأنها حينئذ كانت لا تنعدم مع بقاء الجوهر ، فإذا كان السبب لكونها أغيارا للجوهر امتناع جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء لها، فمن جعل كونها أغيارا له علة لامتناع جعل بقاء الجوهر بقاء للأعراض فقد قلب القصة. لصفاته والذي يحقق فساد ما ذهب إليه الأشعري أن بقاء الله تعالى لو كان بقاء لأنها ليست بأغيار له - للزم أن تكون حياته حياة لذاته ولجميع صفاته، لأنها ليست بأغيار له، وكذا قدرته وعلمه وسمعه وبصره فتصير كل صفة حية قادرة عالمة سميعة بصيرة مريدة، وهذا هو وصف الربوبية فتصير كل صفة موصوفة بالألوهية والربوبية فتؤدي إلى القول بالآلهة وهذا كفر وإبطال للتوحيد.
So the definition of 'two distinct things' is invalid. And since we see that accidents are distinct from the substances in which they inhere, and that they cease to exist while the substance remains, we know that the permanence of the substance is not permanence for the accidents inhering in it. In the case of the unseen, such a proof is absent, except that we say: What al-Ash'ari mentioned is corrupt, because the opponents say: The attribute with you (if it is not the same as the essence), why did you make the permanence subsisting in the essence as permanence for that which is not the essence and for which permanence does not subsist? Do you not see that the permanence of the substance would bring about permanence for its accidents, and it is not so only because the accident is not the substance, not because it is other than it? Rather the description of 'otherness' is a separate matter, because it cannot be said: The permanence of the substance did not become permanence for the accident inhering in it because it is other than it, for it became other than it only because the permanence of the substance did not make it permanent, so it ceased to exist while the substance existed and became other than it. If the permanence of the substance had been made permanence for its accidents, they would not have become other than it, because then they would not cease to exist with the permanence of the substance. So if the reason for their being other than the substance is the impossibility of making the permanence of the substance permanence for them, then whoever makes their being other than it the cause for the impossibility of making the permanence of the substance permanence for the accidents has reversed the story. As for His attributes, what confirms the corruption of what al-Ash'ari held is that if the permanence of God Most High were permanence for them because they are not other than Him, then necessarily His life would be life for His essence and for all His attributes, because they are not other than Him, and likewise His power, knowledge, hearing, sight, will, so every attribute would become living, powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing, willing—and this is the description of lordship, so every attribute would be described with divinity and lordship, leading to belief in multiple gods, and this is disbelief and nullification of monotheism.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ash'ari's argument that the permanence (baqa') of God applies to His attributes. The author first analyzes the relationship between a substance and its accidents: accidents are distinct from the substance; they cease when the substance remains. The permanence of the substance does not grant permanence to its accidents. Then he argues that al-Ash'ari's view is corrupt because if God's permanence were to apply to His attributes (since they are not other than Him), then each attribute would take on the attributes of God—life, power, knowledge, etc.—and thus each attribute would become a god. This leads to polytheism and nullifies tawhid. The author emphasizes that the distinction between accidents and substances is based on the fact that accidents are not made permanent by the substance's permanence; if they were, they would not be 'other'. The argument is that al-Ash'ari has reversed causality by making 'otherness' the reason for not applying permanence, whereas the true reason is that the substance's permanence does not extend to accidents.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa')
الجوهر (jawhar)
العرض ('arad)
الصفة (sifah)
الغير (al-ghayr)
الذات (al-dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's position is about attributes being 'other' than God.
Correction: The refutation shows that if permanence applied to attributes, they would become independent gods, so the issue is not about 'otherness' but about the consequence of applying permanence.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about al-Ash'ari's view on God's permanence applying to His attributes?
A) It is a valid and necessary view.
B) It leads to polytheism and nullifies tawhid.
C) It is only a minor error.
D) It is acceptable but needs clarification.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's claim that God's permanence applies to His attributes is refuted because it would make each attribute a separate deity, destroying tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes like His knowledge or power, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'parts' of God? How does this lesson's warning about polytheism challenge you to purify your understanding of tawhid?
?
This passage shows that a small mistake in reasoning about God can lead to kufr. What are some subtle beliefs or assumptions you hold about God that you have never examined critically, and what step will you take to test them against the Qur'an and sound theology?
?
The author uses a logical argument to defend tawhid. If someone asked you today why believing that God's attributes are 'permanent' in the same way as His essence is dangerous, would you be able to explain it using this lesson? Commit to memorizing one key phrase from the Arabic that captures the proof.

Divine Attributes: Permanence Without Accidental Inherence
The author presents the correct Maturidi answer to the objection against attribute permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi solution to how divine attributes are permanent without requiring an accidental quality of permanence.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are permanent by themselves, not by an accident.

The previous lesson presented an objection that attributes cannot be permanent without an accident of permanence. This lesson presents the Maturidi answer: attributes are inherently permanent; their permanence is self-subsisting. The author builds on several certain premises to reach this conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the unsound answer
Note that the author first rejects a previous answer as unsound. This sets the stage for the correct response.
2
List the established premises
Go through the six premises the author states as established by proof. They form the foundation.
3
Recognize the apparent conflict
The premises seem to conflict when combined, but each is certain. See why the author says they cannot all be denied.
4
Understand the resolution
The resolution is that each attribute is permanent by itself, without a separate meaning inhering in it. This harmonizes the premises.
5
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy of a body being in a place by a specific 'being-in'. This clarifies how something can be permanent by its own permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يستقم هذا الجواب. والذي عليه الاعتماد من الجواب عن هذه الشبهة، وهو أن الدليل قد قام على ثبوت هذه الصفات على وجه يخضع المنصف له، وينقاد ولا يتخالجه ريب ولا شك، وكذا الدليل قام على كون حدوثها ممتنعا محالا. وكذا الدليل قام على استحالة عدمها وحصل العلم بهذه المقدمات أنها دائمة لا محالة، وعلم بالبديهة أن القول بدائم ليس بباق محال، وقد قام الدليل على أن القول بباق لا بقاء له محال. وقد قام دليل امتناع قيام المعنى بالصفة ولا وجه إلى إنكار شيء من هذه المقدمات بثبوت العلم على طريق التيقن بهذه المقدمات، والجمع بين هذه المقدمات في الظاهر ممتنع، ودفع بعضها ممتنع أيضا لقيام دليل ثبوته ولمساواة غيره من المقدمات في صحة الثبوت. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب البحث عما يوجب زوال امتناع ثبوت هذه المقدمات اللاتي ثبت كل واحد منهن بدليل لا مدفع له، فوجدنا ذلك فقلنا بثبوت الصفات لقيام الدليل وببقائها ودوامها وثبوت البقاء لها وامتناع كونها باقية بدون البقاء من غير قيام معنى بها ووجه ذلك أن كل صفات الله تعالى تكون صفة الله تعالى وتكون بقاء لنفسه فيكون العلم علما للذات وكل الذات به عالما، ويكون العلم بقاء لنفسه أيضا فيكون ببقاء هو نفسه. وكذلك بقاء الله تعالى بقاء له وبقاء لنفسه أيضا فيكون الله تعالى به باقيا وهو وبنفسه أيضا باق. هذا كما أن الجسم كائن في مكان بكون يخصه لولا ذلك الكون لما كان.
So this answer is not sound. The reliable answer to this objection is: The proof has been established for the subsistence of these attributes in a way that makes the fair-minded person submit and comply, without any doubt or uncertainty. Likewise, the proof has been established that their origination is impossible and absurd. Likewise, the proof has been established for the impossibility of their non-existence, and knowledge has been obtained from these premises that they are necessarily eternal. It is known by intuition that the statement 'something eternal is not permanent' is absurd. The proof has also been established that the statement 'something permanent has no permanence' is absurd. And there is proof of the impossibility of a meaning inhering in the attribute. There is no way to deny any of these premises, because knowledge is established with certainty regarding these premises. The combination of these premises is apparently impossible, and denying some of them is also impossible because of the proof of their subsistence and the equality of other premises in soundness of subsistence. Since this is the case, it is necessary to seek what removes the impossibility of the subsistence of these premises, each of which is established by a proof that cannot be refuted. So we found that and said: The attributes are established because the proof stands; they are permanent and eternal; permanence is affirmed for them; and it is impossible for them to be permanent without permanence without a meaning inhering in them. The explanation is that every attribute of God Most High is an attribute of God and is permanence for itself, so knowledge is knowledge of the essence and the entire essence is knowing by it; and knowledge is also permanence for itself, so it is by a permanence that is itself. Likewise, the permanence of God Most High is permanence for Him and also permanence for itself, so God Most High is permanent by it and He is also permanent by Himself. This is just as a body is in a place by a 'being-in' (kawn) specific to it; without that 'being-in' it would not be.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage presents the correct answer to the earlier objection regarding the permanence of divine attributes. The author states that the previous answer (presumably al-Ash'ari's) is unsound. The reliable answer is based on several established premises, each proven: (1) The attributes are established in a way that compels acceptance. (2) Their origination is impossible. (3) Their non-existence is impossible. (4) The statement 'something eternal is not permanent' is absurd. (5) The statement 'something permanent has no permanence' is absurd. (6) A meaning (ma'na) cannot inhere in an attribute. These premises cannot be denied because they are certain. Apparently they conflict, so we must seek a resolution. The resolution is that each attribute is both an attribute of God and itself permanent by its own permanence, without a separate accident inhering in it. The author gives an analogy: a body is in a place by a 'being-in' (kawn) that is specific to it; without that 'being-in', the body would not be in the place. Similarly, the attributes are permanent by a permanence that is identical with themselves, not an accidental quality. Thus, the attributes are eternal and have permanence inherently.

Key Terms
البقاء (al-baqa')
الدائم (al-da'im)
المعنى (al-ma'na)
القيام (al-qiyam)
الصفة (al-sifah)
الذات (al-dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying an attribute is 'permanent' implies an accidental permanence inhering in it.
Correction: The author clarifies that the permanence is self-subsisting; the attribute itself is permanence, not requiring a separate accident.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how do divine attributes have permanence without involving accidental inherence?
A) They are permanent by a separate accidental permanence.
B) They are permanent by a permanence that is identical to themselves.
C) They are not really permanent but are called permanent metaphorically.
D) They are permanent only through God's essence, not by themselves.

Key Takeaway
Divine attributes are eternal and inherently permanent, without requiring an accidental quality of permanence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking of God's attributes as temporary or changeable, like human qualities? This lesson says they are permanent by themselves, not by an accident. Does your heart truly accept that God's knowledge, power, and mercy are unchanging and self-subsisting? What would change in your worship today if you fully believed that?
?
The author says these premises are 'established with certainty' and that the fair-minded person submits. Is there any attribute of God that you secretly doubt or feel uncertain about? Pick one attribute (like God's justice or mercy) and commit to meditating on its permanence for five minutes today, asking Allah to remove any doubt.
?
If you truly believed that God's attributes are not accidental but inherent and permanent, how would that affect your trust in His promises? Write down one specific promise from the Quran that you struggle to trust, and reflect on how the permanence of God's truthfulness makes that promise unbreakable.

The Apparent Contradiction in Premises about Attributes
Al-Nasafi enumerates the seemingly contradictory premises about attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the set of premises about divine attributes that seem contradictory, and why none can be denied.

Core Idea
Evidence forces multiple premises that seem impossible to combine.

Al-Nasafi rejects a previous inadequate answer and presents the correct approach to a sophism. He begins by listing the premises that the evidence has established: that attributes exist, that their temporal origination is impossible, that their non-existence is impossible, and that they are eternal. At the same time, it is impossible to say something is eternal without being everlasting, or everlasting without permanence. These premises appear contradictory, yet each is proven and cannot be denied. This sets up the need for a deeper solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the premises
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the sequence of premises and the statement that combining them superficially is impossible.
This passage is the problem statement.
2
Identify each premise
List the premises: existence of attributes, impossibility of temporal origination, impossibility of non-existence, necessity of eternity, impossibility of 'eternal without everlasting', impossibility of 'everlasting without permanence'.
There are at least five premises.
3
Understand the dilemma
The author says combining them is impossible, but rejecting any is also impossible because each has evidence. This creates a rational impasse that demands a resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يستقم هذا الجواب. والذي عليه الاعتماد من الجواب عن هذه الشبهة، وهو أن الدليل قد قام على ثبوت هذه الصفات على وجه يخضع المنصف له، وينقاد ولا يتخالجه ريب ولا شك، وكذا الدليل قام على كون حدوثها ممتنعا محالا. وكذا الدليل قام على استحالة عدمها وحصل العلم بهذه المقدمات أنها دائمة لا محالة، وعلم بالبديهة أن القول بدائم ليس بباق محال، وقد قام الدليل على أن القول بباق لا بقاء له محال. وقد قام دليل امتناع قيام المعنى بالصفة ولا وجه إلى إنكار شيء من هذه المقدمات بثبوت العلم على طريق التيقن بهذه المقدمات، والجمع بين هذه المقدمات في الظاهر ممتنع، ودفع بعضها ممتنع أيضا لقيام دليل ثبوته ولمساواة غيره من المقدمات في صحة الثبوت. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب البحث عما يوجب زوال امتناع ثبوت هذه المقدمات اللاتي ثبت كل واحد منهن بدليل لا مدفع له، فوجدنا ذلك فقلنا بثبوت الصفات لقيام الدليل وببقائها ودوامها وثبوت البقاء لها وامتناع كونها باقية بدون البقاء من غير قيام معنى بها ووجه ذلك أن كل صفات الله تعالى تكون صفة الله تعالى وتكون بقاء لنفسه فيكون العلم علما للذات وكل الذات به عالما، ويكون العلم بقاء لنفسه أيضا فيكون ببقاء هو نفسه. وكذلك بقاء الله تعالى بقاء له وبقاء لنفسه أيضا فيكون الله تعالى به باقيا وهو وبنفسه أيضا باق. هذا كما أن الجسم كائن في مكان بكون يخصه لولا ذلك الكون لما كان مختصا بهذا المكان المتعين، وهذا لكونه معنى زائدا على ذات الجسم غير راجع إلى الذات فإنه كان موجودا قبل هذا ولا تمكن له بهذا المكان، ثم يزول التمكن وذاته موجود والعرض وهو السواد القائم بمحل كائن في هذا المحل المخصوص مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به، فإن الله تعالى كان عالما بهذا السواد في حالة العدم، وكان قادرا أن يوجده في محل آخر فإذا اختص بهذا مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به كان كائنا ولابد من أن يكون كائنا بكونه، واستحال قيام كون هو غير ذاته بذاته فكان كائنا بكون هو نفسه، وكذا كون الجسم في مكان كان كائنا في الجسم لقيامه به مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به ولا وجه إلى القول بكونه كائنا بكون قائم به فكان قائما بكون هو نفسه، إذ محال أن يكون كائنا بلا بكون فكذا هذا، والإيضاح بنظير الكون للأجسام والأعراض، وإثبات كون العرض راجعا إلى ذاته. وذهب إليه بعض أصحاب الصفات منهم أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني ذكره في كتابه المسمى بتعجيز المعتزلة وغيره من الكتب وبدون الإيضاح بالنظير يثبت صحة هذا الكلام لما مر من قيام الأدلة المثبتة لذلك.
That answer was not sound. The reliable answer to this sophism is that the evidence has been established for the existence of these attributes in a way that the fair-minded person submits to, yields, and is not disturbed by doubt or uncertainty. Likewise, the evidence has been established that their temporal origination is impossible. Likewise, the evidence has been established for the impossibility of their non-existence, and knowledge has been attained from these premises that they are necessarily eternal. And it is known by intuition that the statement 'eternal without being everlasting (baqin)' is impossible. And evidence has been established that the statement 'everlasting without having permanence (baqa')' is impossible. And evidence has been established for the impossibility of a meaning subsisting in an attribute. There is no way to deny any of these premises, since knowledge is established in a manner of certainty about these premises. Superficially, combining these premises is impossible, and rejecting some of them is also impossible because evidence for their existence stands and because others of these premises are equal in the validity of existence. When this is the case, it is necessary to search for what removes the impossibility of establishing these premises, each of which is established by an irrefutable proof. So we found that and said: [We affirm] the existence of the attributes due to the evidence, their permanence (baqa'), their eternity, the establishment of permanence (baqa') for them, and the impossibility of them being everlasting (baqiyah) without permanence (baqa') without a meaning subsisting in them. The explanation of that is: every attribute of God Most High is an attribute of God Most High and is a permanence (baqa') for itself. Thus knowledge is knowledge for the essence, and the entire essence is knowing through it. And knowledge is also a permanence for itself, so it is through a permanence that is itself. Likewise, the permanence (baqa') of God Most High is a permanence for Him and a permanence for itself also. Thus God Most High is everlasting (baqin) through it, and He is also everlasting through Himself. This is like a body being in a place by a specific 'being-in' (kawn). Without that 'being-in', it would not be confined to that specific place. This is because it is an additional meaning beyond the body's essence, not reducible to the essence, for it existed before this and had no location in this place, then the location ceases while its essence exists. And the accident, like blackness subsisting in a subject, is a 'being-in' (ka'in) in this specific subject, despite the possibility that it might not be confined to it. Indeed, God Most High knew this blackness in the state of non-existence and was able to create it in another subject. When it becomes confined to this [subject], despite the possibility that it might not be confined to it, it is a 'being-in' (ka'in), and it must be a 'being-in' by its 'being-in' (kawn). It is impossible for a 'being-in' that is not its own essence to subsist by its essence, so it is a 'being-in' by a 'being-in' that is itself. Similarly, the 'being-in' of the body in a place is a 'being-in' in the body because it subsists in it, despite the possibility that it might not be confined to it. There is no way to say that it is a 'being-in' by a 'being-in' subsisting in it, so it is subsisting by a 'being-in' that is itself, since it is impossible for it to be a 'being-in' without a 'being-in'. So this is the case. And clarification by analogy with the 'being-in' (kawn) of bodies and accidents, and the establishment that the 'being-in' of the accident is reducible to its essence. Some of the proponents of attributes held this, among them Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, as mentioned in his book titled 'Ta'jiz al-Mu'tazila' and other books. And without clarification by analogy, the soundness of this statement is established based on what has preceded of the evidence that establishes that.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage presents the reliable answer to a sophism (shubha) concerning the divine attributes. The sophism arises from apparently contradictory premises: the evidence proves that attributes exist, that their temporal origination is impossible, that their non-existence is impossible, and that they are eternal; yet it also proves that saying 'eternal without being everlasting' is impossible, and saying 'everlasting without permanence' is impossible. These premises seem incompatible, but none can be denied. The author resolves this by affirming that attributes have permanence (baqa') both for the divine essence and for themselves. He explains that each attribute (e.g., knowledge) is an attribute of God and also a permanence for itself, so through it the essence knows and through it the attribute subsists. He then provides an analogy: a body's being in a place requires an additional meaning (kawn) that is not the body's essence; similarly, an accident's existence in a subject requires a specific relation. Just as the accident's 'being-in' (kawn) is reducible to its essence, so the attribute's permanence is both for the essence and for itself. The author notes that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held this view, and that the argument stands even without the analogy due to the established proofs.

صفات
دليل
بقاء
باق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the premises are truly contradictory and thus some must be false.
Correction: The premises are all true; the apparent contradiction is resolved by a deeper understanding of how attributes relate to the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot any of the premises be rejected?
A) Because they are all intuitively obvious
B) Because each is established by irrefutable proof and they are equal in validity
C) Because rejecting one would lead to a contradiction
D) Because the author says so without reason

Key Takeaway
The premises about attributes are individually proven but appear contradictory, necessitating a resolution that does not deny any.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a truth about Allah that seems to contradict another truth you hold, do you instinctively reject one to ease your mind? Al-Nasafi insists we must not deny any. What will you do the next time you face such an apparent contradiction?
?
You claim to believe in an eternal God with attributes. Have you ever examined your own understanding of those attributes beyond surface-level acceptance? What if your unexamined assumptions contain hidden contradictions? Will you seek certain knowledge like the fair-minded person described?
?
The lesson says knowledge is established with certainty about these premises—yet how many of your beliefs about Allah are based on certainty versus mere acceptance? What one step can you take today to turn a casual belief into a certain conviction?

Divine Attributes

The Solution: Attributes as Permanence for Themselves
Al-Nasafi presents the solution to the shubha by affirming attributes as self-subsisting permanence, with analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how affirming that each attribute is a permanence for itself resolves the apparent contradiction among the premises.

Core Idea
Attributes are both divine attributes and self-subsisting permanence.

Having established the problem, Al-Nasafi now presents the solution. He affirms that attributes exist, are everlasting, and have permanence (baqa') in a way that does not require a separate meaning subsisting in them. Instead, each attribute (like knowledge) is a permanence for itself: it makes the essence knowing and is itself subsisting. He uses an analogy: a body's being in a place requires a 'kawn' that is not the body's essence; similarly, an accident's existence in a subject has a 'kawn' that is reducible to the accident itself. This illustrates how attributes can be both for the essence and for themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the solution passage
Begin from 'فوجدنا ذلك فقلنا...' and read through to the end. Notice the key statement: attributes are both divine attributes and permanence for themselves.
This is the core of the answer.
2
Analyze the example of knowledge
The author explains that knowledge is a permanence for itself, so the essence knows through it and it subsists through itself. Understand how this resolves the impossibility of a meaning subsisting in an attribute.
3
Study the analogy with bodies and accidents
The analogy shows how a body's 'being-in' (kawn) is additional to its essence but not separable; similarly, the attribute's permanence is not a separate accident but identical to the attribute itself.
This is a sophisticated point; take time to grasp it.
4
Consider the conclusion
The author notes that this view was held by Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini and that the argument stands even without the analogy, due to the earlier proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يستقم هذا الجواب. والذي عليه الاعتماد من الجواب عن هذه الشبهة، وهو أن الدليل قد قام على ثبوت هذه الصفات على وجه يخضع المنصف له، وينقاد ولا يتخالجه ريب ولا شك، وكذا الدليل قام على كون حدوثها ممتنعا محالا. وكذا الدليل قام على استحالة عدمها وحصل العلم بهذه المقدمات أنها دائمة لا محالة، وعلم بالبديهة أن القول بدائم ليس بباق محال، وقد قام الدليل على أن القول بباق لا بقاء له محال. وقد قام دليل امتناع قيام المعنى بالصفة ولا وجه إلى إنكار شيء من هذه المقدمات بثبوت العلم على طريق التيقن بهذه المقدمات، والجمع بين هذه المقدمات في الظاهر ممتنع، ودفع بعضها ممتنع أيضا لقيام دليل ثبوته ولمساواة غيره من المقدمات في صحة الثبوت. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب البحث عما يوجب زوال امتناع ثبوت هذه المقدمات اللاتي ثبت كل واحد منهن بدليل لا مدفع له، فوجدنا ذلك فقلنا بثبوت الصفات لقيام الدليل وببقائها ودوامها وثبوت البقاء لها وامتناع كونها باقية بدون البقاء من غير قيام معنى بها ووجه ذلك أن كل صفات الله تعالى تكون صفة الله تعالى وتكون بقاء لنفسه فيكون العلم علما للذات وكل الذات به عالما، ويكون العلم بقاء لنفسه أيضا فيكون ببقاء هو نفسه. وكذلك بقاء الله تعالى بقاء له وبقاء لنفسه أيضا فيكون الله تعالى به باقيا وهو وبنفسه أيضا باق. هذا كما أن الجسم كائن في مكان بكون يخصه لولا ذلك الكون لما كان مختصا بهذا المكان المتعين، وهذا لكونه معنى زائدا على ذات الجسم غير راجع إلى الذات فإنه كان موجودا قبل هذا ولا تمكن له بهذا المكان، ثم يزول التمكن وذاته موجود والعرض وهو السواد القائم بمحل كائن في هذا المحل المخصوص مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به، فإن الله تعالى كان عالما بهذا السواد في حالة العدم، وكان قادرا أن يوجده في محل آخر فإذا اختص بهذا مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به كان كائنا ولابد من أن يكون كائنا بكونه، واستحال قيام كون هو غير ذاته بذاته فكان كائنا بكون هو نفسه، وكذا كون الجسم في مكان كان كائنا في الجسم لقيامه به مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به ولا وجه إلى القول بكونه كائنا بكون قائم به فكان قائما بكون هو نفسه، إذ محال أن يكون كائنا بلا بكون فكذا هذا، والإيضاح بنظير الكون للأجسام والأعراض، وإثبات كون العرض راجعا إلى ذاته. وذهب إليه بعض أصحاب الصفات منهم أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني ذكره في كتابه المسمى بتعجيز المعتزلة وغيره من الكتب وبدون الإيضاح بالنظير يثبت صحة هذا الكلام لما مر من قيام الأدلة المثبتة لذلك.
That answer was not sound. The reliable answer to this sophism is that the evidence has been established for the existence of these attributes in a way that the fair-minded person submits to, yields, and is not disturbed by doubt or uncertainty. Likewise, the evidence has been established that their temporal origination is impossible. Likewise, the evidence has been established for the impossibility of their non-existence, and knowledge has been attained from these premises that they are necessarily eternal. And it is known by intuition that the statement 'eternal without being everlasting (baqin)' is impossible. And evidence has been established that the statement 'everlasting without having permanence (baqa')' is impossible. And evidence has been established for the impossibility of a meaning subsisting in an attribute. There is no way to deny any of these premises, since knowledge is established in a manner of certainty about these premises. Superficially, combining these premises is impossible, and rejecting some of them is also impossible because evidence for their existence stands and because others of these premises are equal in the validity of existence. When this is the case, it is necessary to search for what removes the impossibility of establishing these premises, each of which is established by an irrefutable proof. So we found that and said: [We affirm] the existence of the attributes due to the evidence, their permanence (baqa'), their eternity, the establishment of permanence (baqa') for them, and the impossibility of them being everlasting (baqiyah) without permanence (baqa') without a meaning subsisting in them. The explanation of that is: every attribute of God Most High is an attribute of God Most High and is a permanence (baqa') for itself. Thus knowledge is knowledge for the essence, and the entire essence is knowing through it. And knowledge is also a permanence for itself, so it is through a permanence that is itself. Likewise, the permanence (baqa') of God Most High is a permanence for Him and a permanence for itself also. Thus God Most High is everlasting (baqin) through it, and He is also everlasting through Himself. This is like a body being in a place by a specific 'being-in' (kawn). Without that 'being-in', it would not be confined to that specific place. This is because it is an additional meaning beyond the body's essence, not reducible to the essence, for it existed before this and had no location in this place, then the location ceases while its essence exists. And the accident, like blackness subsisting in a subject, is a 'being-in' (ka'in) in this specific subject, despite the possibility that it might not be confined to it. Indeed, God Most High knew this blackness in the state of non-existence and was able to create it in another subject. When it becomes confined to this [subject], despite the possibility that it might not be confined to it, it is a 'being-in' (ka'in), and it must be a 'being-in' by its 'being-in' (kawn). It is impossible for a 'being-in' that is not its own essence to subsist by its essence, so it is a 'being-in' by a 'being-in' that is itself. Similarly, the 'being-in' of the body in a place is a 'being-in' in the body because it subsists in it, despite the possibility that it might not be confined to it. There is no way to say that it is a 'being-in' by a 'being-in' subsisting in it, so it is subsisting by a 'being-in' that is itself, since it is impossible for it to be a 'being-in' without a 'being-in'. So this is the case. And clarification by analogy with the 'being-in' (kawn) of bodies and accidents, and the establishment that the 'being-in' of the accident is reducible to its essence. Some of the proponents of attributes held this, among them Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, as mentioned in his book titled 'Ta'jiz al-Mu'tazila' and other books. And without clarification by analogy, the soundness of this statement is established based on what has preceded of the evidence that establishes that.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage presents the reliable answer to a sophism (shubha) concerning the divine attributes. The sophism arises from apparently contradictory premises: the evidence proves that attributes exist, that their temporal origination is impossible, that their non-existence is impossible, and that they are eternal; yet it also proves that saying 'eternal without being everlasting' is impossible, and saying 'everlasting without permanence' is impossible. These premises seem incompatible, but none can be denied. The author resolves this by affirming that attributes have permanence (baqa') both for the divine essence and for themselves. He explains that each attribute (e.g., knowledge) is an attribute of God and also a permanence for itself, so through it the essence knows and through it the attribute subsists. He then provides an analogy: a body's being in a place requires an additional meaning (kawn) that is not the body's essence; similarly, an accident's existence in a subject requires a specific relation. Just as the accident's 'being-in' (kawn) is reducible to its essence, so the attribute's permanence is both for the essence and for itself. The author notes that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held this view, and that the argument stands even without the analogy due to the established proofs.

Key Terms
صفات
بقاء
كون
باق
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does knowledge exist as an attribute of God?
A) It subsists as a separate accident in God's essence
B) It is both knowledge of the essence and a permanence for itself
C) It is a meaning (ma'na) that inheres in God
D) It is identical to God's essence without any distinction

Key Takeaway
Affirming that each attribute is a permanence for itself resolves the apparent contradictions and does not require a separate meaning subsisting in the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attributes are self-subsisting permanences, do you truly entrust your life to His unchanging knowledge and power, or do you live as if He might change His mind or forget?
?
How does the fact that God's attribute of mercy is a permanence for itself challenge the moments when you feel undeserving of His mercy – do you still turn to Him expecting it?
?

Qualifying the Impossibility of Two Things Sharing One Permanence
Al-Nasafi qualifies the impossibility of two things sharing one permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Nasafi qualifies the impossibility of two things sharing one attribute, and why the objection against baqa' fails.

Core Idea
Not all cases of two things sharing one attribute are impossible.

Al-Nasafi now responds to a logical objection against the doctrine of baqa'. The objector claims that if baqa' is a permanence for the essence and also for itself, then two things would share one baqa', which is impossible. But Al-Nasafi shows that this impossibility depends on the nature of the things involved. This lesson unpacks his qualification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the objection as stated in the Arabic passage. Notice the claim that two everlasting things cannot share one permanence, citing analogies of motion and blackness.
2
Identify the author's qualification
The author begins his answer by stating that the impossibility only applies under a specific condition: when one of the two things cannot be a permanence for itself and only becomes everlasting through another permanence. This is the key point.
3
Apply the qualification to the objection
Consider how this qualification undermines the objection. If the attribute's baqa' is self-subsisting, then the essence and the attribute can share the same baqa' without absurdity. This shows the objection is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال بأن البقاء إذا جعل بقاء للذات يستحيل أن يكون بقاء لنفسه، لأنه يؤدي إلى القول بحصول الباقيين ببقاء واحد وهو محال، فإن حصول متحركين بحركة واحدة وأسودين بسواد واحد محال لأن نقول بأن حصول باقيين ببقاء واحد إنما يستحيل إذا كان أحد الباقيين مما يستحيل أن يكون بقاء لنفسه ولا يصير باقيا إلا بقيام البقاء
And it should not be said that if permanence (al-baqa') is made a permanence for the essence, it is impossible for it to be a permanence for itself, because that leads to the statement that two everlasting things (baqiyayn) occur by one permanence (baqa'), which is impossible. For the occurrence of two moving things by one motion and two black things by one blackness is impossible, because we say that the occurrence of two everlasting things by one permanence is only impossible if one of the two everlasting things is such that it is impossible for it to be a permanence for itself and it does not become everlasting except by the subsistence of permanence.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of its refutation. The objection states: if we say that baqa' (permanence) is a permanence for the essence (i.e., God's essence is everlasting by it) and also a permanence for itself (i.e., baqa' itself is everlasting), then two things (the essence and the attribute) would be everlasting by one and the same baqa', which is impossible, just as two moving things cannot share one motion or two black things one blackness. The author begins his answer by qualifying the impossibility: it is only impossible if one of the two everlasting things cannot be a permanence for itself and only becomes everlasting through the subsistence of another baqa'. This implies that if one of the things (the attribute's baqa') is self-subsisting, then the two can share the same baqa' without absurdity. The full answer would continue, but the text breaks off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the impossibility of two things sharing one attribute is absolute and unconditional.
Correction: The text shows that the impossibility depends on the nature of the things involved; if one is self-subsisting, sharing is not impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is it impossible for two everlasting things to occur by one permanence?
A) Always, without exception
B) When one of them cannot be a permanence for itself and only becomes everlasting by another permanence
C) When both are attributes of God
D) When the permanence is not additional to the essence

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two things sharing one attribute is not absolute; it only applies when one of them cannot exist independently.

The Kawn of an Accident is Its Own Being
Argument for kawn being self-identical for accidents and bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specific existence of an accident (like blackness) in a subject requires a kawn that is identical to its own essence, not a separate attribute.

Core Idea
An accident's 'being' in its subject is nothing other than itself.

This lesson examines a key argument in kalam about the nature of 'being' (kawn) for accidents and bodies. The author argues that when an accident like blackness exists in a particular subject, its 'being' there is not a separate accident but its own essence. This has significant implications for understanding how attributes subsist in subjects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on blackness
Carefully read the first part where the author discusses blackness as an accident. Notice how he argues that blackness is 'being' (kā'in) in a specific subject despite the possibility of being elsewhere.
Focus on the phrase 'كان كائنا بكونه' – it is key.
2
Identify the conclusion for accidents
After the argument, the author concludes that the accident is 'being' by a kawn that is itself. Mark this conclusion.
3
Examine the analogy with the body
Read how the same reasoning applies to a body's existence in a place. The body's kawn is also itself.
The author uses 'فكذا هذا' to draw the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مختصا بهذا المكان المتعين ، وهذا لكونه معنى زائدا على ذات الجسم غير راجع إلى الذات فإنه كان موجودا قبل هذا ولا تمكن له بهذا المكان، ثم يزول التمكن وذاته موجود والعرض وهو السواد القائم بمحل كائن في هذا المحل المخصوص مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به، فإن الله تعالى كان عالما بهذا السواد في حالة العدم، وكان قادرا أن يوجده في محل آخر فإذا اختص بهذا مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به كان كائنا ولابد من أن يكون كائنا بكونه، واستحال قيام كون هو غير ذاته بذاته فكان كائنا بكون هو نفسه، وكذا كون الجسم في مكان كان كائنا في الجسم لقيامه به مع جواز أن لا يكون مختصا به ولا وجه إلى القول بكونه كائنا بكون قائم به فكان قائما بكون هو نفسه، إذ محال أن يكون كائنا بلا كون فكذا هذا، والإيضاح بنظير الكون للأجسام والأعراض، وإثبات كون العرض راجعا إلى ذاته. وذهب إليه بعض أصحاب الصفات منهم أبو إسحاق الأسفراييني ذكره في كتابه المسمى بتعجيز المعتزلة وغيره من الكتب وبدون الإيضاح بالنظير يثبت صحة هذا الكلام لما مر من قيام الأدلة المثبتة لذلك. ولا يقال بأن البقاء إذا جعل بقاء للذات يستحيل أن يكون بقاء لنفسه، لأنه يؤدي إلى القول بحصول الباقيين ببقاء واحد وهو محال، فإن حصول متحركين بحركة واحدة وأسودين بسواد واحد محال لأن نقول بأن حصول باقيين ببقاء واحد إنما يستحيل إذا كان أحد الباقيين مما يستحيل أن يكون بقاء لنفسه ولا يصير باقيا إلا بقيام البقاء به، وقيام بقاء واحد بذاتين محال، فاستحال صيرورتهما باقيين ببقاء واحد، فأما إذا كان أحد الباقيين يكون بقاء لنفسه ثم يقول بالباقي الآخر، كان كل واحد منها باقيا ولم يستحل ذلك، لأنه لم يؤد إلى قيام بقاء بذاتين وهو علة الاستحالة واعتبر بكون الجسم فإن الجسم يكون به كائنا والكون نفسه يكون كائنا بكون هو نفسه ولم يكن ذلك محال وكذا هذا، ويعرف ذلك بضرورة الدليل.
Specified to this particular place, and this is because it is an additional meaning to the essence of the body not reducible to the essence, because it existed before this and there is no possibility for it in this place, then the possibility ceases while its essence exists. The accident, which is blackness subsisting in a subject, is 'being' (kā'in) in this particular subject with the possibility that it is not specified to it. For God was knowing of this blackness in the state of non-existence, and was able to bring it into existence in another subject. So when it becomes specified to this [subject] with the possibility that it is not specified to it, it is 'being' (kā'in), and it must be that it is 'being' by its 'being' (kawn). It is impossible for a 'being' (kawn) that is other than its essence to subsist in its essence, so it is 'being' by a 'being' that is itself. Likewise, the 'being' (kawn) of a body in a place: the body is 'being' in it because of its subsistence in it, with the possibility that it is not specified to it, and there is no way to say that it is 'being' by a 'being' subsisting in it, so it is subsistent by a 'being' that is itself, for it is impossible for something to be 'being' without a 'being'. So it is with this. Clarification by the analogue of 'being' (kawn) for bodies and accidents, and proving that the 'being' of an accident is reducible to its essence. Some of the masters of attributes held this, among them Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, as mentioned in his book titled 'Ta'jiz al-Mu'tazila' and other books. Even without clarification by analogue, the correctness of this speech is established by what has passed of the proofs establishing that. And it should not be said that when al-baqā' (duration/remaining) is made a duration for the essence, it is impossible for it to be a duration for itself, because that leads to saying that two enduring things (bāqiyān) exist by one duration, which is impossible. For the existence of two moving things by one motion and two black things by one blackness is impossible. Because we say that the existence of two enduring things by one duration is only impossible if one of the two enduring things is such that it is impossible for it to be a duration for itself and it only becomes enduring by the subsistence of duration in it, and the subsistence of one duration in two essences is impossible. So their becoming two enduring things by one duration is impossible. However, if one of the two enduring things is a duration for itself, and then one speaks of the other enduring thing, each one of them is enduring and that is not impossible, because it does not lead to the subsistence of one duration in two essences, which is the cause of impossibility. Consider by analogy the 'being' (kawn) of the body: the body is 'being' by it, and the 'being' itself is 'being' by a 'being' that is itself, and that was not impossible. And this is similar. And that is known by the necessity of proof.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage presents a complex argument about the nature of 'being' (kawn) and 'remaining' (baqā'). The author begins by discussing an accident (like blackness) that is 'specified' (mukhtaṣṣ) to a particular subject. He argues that the blackness's 'being in that subject' is an additional meaning (ma'nā) beyond its essence, but it is not reducible to its essence because it could have been in another place. However, when it is in that particular place, it is 'being' (kā'in) by a 'being' (kawn) that is itself, because it is impossible for a being that is not itself to subsist in it. The same applies to a body's being in a place: the body's spatial existence is due to a 'being' that is itself, not a separate accident. Then the author mentions that Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held this view and that it can be established without the analogy. Next, he addresses an objection: if durance (baqā') is made a durance for the essence, it cannot be a durance for itself, because that would imply two enduring things sharing one durance, which is impossible (like two moving things sharing one motion). The answer is that this impossibility only occurs when both enduring things are not durance by themselves and require an external durance subsisting in them. But if one of them is durance for itself, then the other can be enduring by that same durance without it subsisting in two essences. He uses the analogy of 'being' (kawn): the body is 'being' by a kawn, and that kawn itself is 'being' by itself, and that is not impossible. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
كون (kawn)
ذات (dhāt)
عرض ('araḍ)
محل (maḥall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the kawn of an accident is a separate accident (like a 'beingness' attribute) that subsists in the subject.
Correction: The author shows that such a view leads to an infinite regress or impossibility, so the kawn must be the accident's own essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for the kawn of blackness to be a separate accident?
A) Because God's knowledge prevents it
B) Because that would require the kawn to subsist in blackness, but blackness is an accident and cannot support another accident
C) Because the blackness was not in that place before, so its kawn is new
D) Because the kawn would then be 'being' by another kawn, leading to infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The specific existence of a thing in a place is not due to a separate attribute but is identical to the thing's own being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your essence is not separate from your existence in this moment. God knows you before you existed and could have placed you elsewhere. Do you truly believe that your specific life – your family, your struggles, your location – is not an accident but your very self? How would your gratitude change if you owned this truth fully? This hour, thank God for specifying you exactly where you are.
?
The accident could have been in another subject, but God chose this one. Have you resented the 'place' God has put you in – your job, your health, your circumstances? This lesson says your being in that place is not a separate misfortune but identical to you. Will you now accept that your situation is not something added to you, but who you are before God? Commit to embracing one specific circumstance you have been resisting as part of your very essence.
?
If your 'being' here is not by a separate attribute but by yourself, then your dependence on God is more intimate than you imagined. He does not just give you attributes; He gives you your very existence. Does this thought pierce your self-sufficiency? When you feel independent, remember the blackness: its existence is a direct act of God. Will you, today, recite 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (39:62) with the awareness that even your location is created directly?
